Causality and Created Things
intermediate
5 min
The Distinction Between Resolve and Intention in Created Will
Enumeration of sectarian views on created will and argument distinguishing resolve from intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why many theologians distinguish between resolve ('azm) and intention (qasd) in the debate over whether created will necessitates its object.

Core Idea
Only intention (qasd), not resolve ('azm), can necessitate the act.

This passage explores a crucial distinction in kalam about human will. While some early Mu'tazilites argued that any created will necessitates its object, others—including the Ash'aris—disagreed. The commentator presents the argument that resolve, which can precede the act and may vanish, cannot be causative, while immediate intention is simultaneous with the act and thus can necessitate. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main positions
Read the passage and note the two groups: the Ash'aris and later Mu'tazilites who deny necessitation, and al-Nazzam's group who allow it only for intention (qasd).
2
Understand the definition of resolve
Resolve ('azm) is defined as the steadying of the soul after hesitation. It can vary in intensity and may reach certainty (jazzm) yet still precede the act.
3
Follow the argument against resolve's causality
The argument: resolve can be non-simultaneous, it can vanish, so even at its strongest it doesn't guarantee the act. Therefore it cannot necessitate.
This is the core of the distinction.
4
Note the Ash'ari conclusion
The Ash'aris go further: resolve is not even a form of will; it is something different. This completely severs the causal link.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وأما ) الإرادة ( الحادثة، فلا توجبه اتفاقاً يعني أن إرادة أحدنا إذا تعلقت بفعل من أفعاله فإنها لا توجب ذلك المراد عند الأشاعرة، وإن كانت مقارنة له عندهم، ووافقهم في ذلك الجبائي وابنه وجماعة من متأخري المعتزلة ( وجوزه النظام ) والعلاف وجعفر بن حارث وطائفة من قدماء معتزلة البصرة ( إيجابها أي إيجاب الإرادة الحادثة ( للمراد إذا كانت تلك الإرادة قصداً إلى الفعل وهو ) أي القصد إلى الفعل ( ما نجده من أنفسنا حال الإيجاد ) أي حال إيجادنا للفعل ( لا عزماً عليه ) لأن الإرادة إذا كانت عزماً على الفعل لم توجب المراد ) فإنه قد يتقدم ) العزم ( على الفعل)، فلا يتصور إيجابه إياه واستدلوا على ذلك بأن العزم توطين النفس على أحد الأمرين بعد سابقة التردد فيهما ( والعزم ) الذي هو التوطين ( يقبل الشدة والضعف ) ويتقوى شيئاً فشيئاً ( حتى يبلغ إلى درجة الجزم) فيزول التردد بالكلية (ومع ذلك فقد لا يكون) العزم الواصل إلى مرتبة الجزم ( مقارناً) للفعل ) ولا قصداً) إليه (بل) يكون ( جزماً بأنه سيقصد ) الفعل فيكون متقدماً على الفعل لغير موجب له ( وربما يزول ) ذلك العزم أي الجزم ( لزوال شرط ) من شرائط الفعل ( أو حدوث مانع من موانعه، فلا يوجد الفعل بعده أيضاً، وإذا لم يكن التوطين البالغ حد الجزم موجباً للفعل، فالذي لم يبلغه كان أولى بعدم الإيجاب فهؤلاء أثبتوا إرادة وتقدمة على الفعل بأزمنة هي العزم ولم يجوزوا كونها موجبة القصد وجوزوا إيجابها إياه، وأما الأشاعرة فلم يجعلوا العزم من قبيل الإرادة بل أمراً مغايراً لها .
(As for) the temporal will, it does not necessitate it by agreement, meaning that the will of any one of us, if it attaches to an act of his own, does not necessitate that willed object according to the Ash'aris, even though it is simultaneous with it according to them. And al-Jubba'i, his son, and a group of later Mu'tazilites agreed with them on that. And al-Nazzam, al-'Allaf, Ja'far ibn Harith, and a group of the early Mu'tazilites of Basra regarded it as permissible (that it necessitates), i.e., the necessitation of the temporal will for the willed object, when that will is an intention (qasd) towards the act, and it (i.e., the intention towards the act) is what we find in ourselves at the moment of bringing into existence, not a resolve ('azm) upon it, because if will is a resolve upon the act, it does not necessitate the willed object, for resolve may precede the act, so its necessitation of it is inconceivable. And they argued for that by the fact that resolve is the steadying of the soul upon one of two things after prior hesitation in them, and resolve (which is steadying) accepts intensity and weakness, and it strengthens little by little until it reaches the degree of certainty (jazzm), then hesitation disappears entirely. But despite that, the resolve that has reached the degree of certainty may not be simultaneous with the act nor an intention towards it, but rather is a certainty that he will intend the act, so it precedes the act without a cause for it. And perhaps it (i.e., that resolve, i.e., certainty) ceases due to the disappearance of a condition of the act or the occurrence of an obstacle among its obstacles, so the act does not occur afterwards either. And if the steadying that has reached the limit of certainty is not causative of the act, then what has not reached it is even more deserving of not causing. So these people affirmed a will that precedes the act by times, which is resolve, and they did not allow it to be causative of intention, but they allowed its necessitation of it. As for the Ash'aris, they did not consider resolve to be of the category of will, but rather something different from it.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage discusses the temporal will (al-irāda al-ḥāditha) and whether it necessitates its object. The commentator reports that, by agreement among Ash'aris, the temporal will of a human does not necessitate the willed act; it is merely simultaneous with it (muqārina). Al-Jubba'i, his son, and later Mu'tazilites agreed. However, al-Nazzam, al-'Allaf, Ja'far ibn Harith, and early Basran Mu'tazilites held that the temporal will does necessitate the act when it is intention (qasd) toward the action, which is what we find in ourselves at the moment of producing the act, not mere resolve ('azm). The argument distinguishing resolve from intention is then explained: resolve is the steadying of the soul after hesitation, which can vary in intensity, and even when it reaches certainty (jazzm), it may not be simultaneous with the act; it can precede the act and may later vanish if conditions change or obstacles arise. Therefore, resolve does not necessitate the act. In contrast, intention (qasd) is simultaneous with the act and thus can be considered causative. The Ash'aris, however, deny that resolve is at all a form of will; they consider it something entirely different.

Key Terms
الإرادة الحادثة
القصد
العزم
الجزم
التوطين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All forms of created will necessarily cause their objects.
Correction: The passage shows that only intention (qasd), which is simultaneous with the act, can necessitate; resolve ('azm), which may precede and vanish, does not.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does resolve ('azm) not necessitate the act?
A) Because resolve is always weak and never leads to action.
B) Because resolve can precede the act and may disappear before the act occurs.
C) Because resolve is identical to intention and intention does not necessitate.
D) Because resolve is only found in God, not in humans.

Key Takeaway
The causal efficacy of created will depends on its type: only intention (qasd), which is simultaneous with the act, can necessitate; resolve ('azm) lacks the necessary simultaneity and can vanish, so it does not necessitate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you made a firm resolve to do something good but never acted on it, what was the real obstacle? How does this lesson reveal the weakness of relying solely on resolve rather than cultivating intention at the moment of action? Will you change anything about how you prepare for acts of worship?
?
Your intention (qasd) must be present at the very moment you act. Are your intentions often scattered or weak when you actually perform a deed? What would it mean for you to train yourself to be fully present in your intention when you pray, give charity, or help someone?
?
If even the most certain human resolve can vanish due to a missing condition or an obstacle, how does that affect your trust in your own planning? Are you more likely to rely on God's help to sustain your intention until the moment of action, or do you trust your own firmness of will? How will you remind yourself of this dependence?

Why the Argument for Mutual Impediment is Necessary
Refutation of the claim that the argument for tamānu' is unnecessary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument for mutual impediment (tamānu') is necessary even if two powers could theoretically cooperate.

Core Idea
Two powers can cooperate, but only the possibility of conflict proves the need for tamānu'.

This passage addresses a challenge to the classical kalam argument from mutual impediment. Some argued that the argument is redundant because two causes on one effect is impossible. The commentator shows that cooperation is possible, so the argument must still be made to exclude that scenario.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: objection then response.
2
Identify the objection
The objector claims that tamānu' is unnecessary because if all things come from God, then one act by the servant would imply two causes on one effect, which is impossible. Understand why this seems to make tamānu' redundant.
3
Understand the response
The commentator rejects this by showing that cooperation is possible: two powers willing the same thing can jointly produce an effect, like two people carrying a plank. Grasp this analogy.
This position is attributed to Abu Ishaq.
4
Connect to the need for tamānu'
Since cooperation is possible, the argument for tamānu' is still needed to consider what happens when the two powers will opposite things. That is the real point of the argument.
Without tamānu', we cannot disprove the possibility of cooperation.
5
Reflect on the doctrine
Consider how this relates to the broader discourse on human free will and divine power in kalam. Why does this matter?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فلو أراد إلخ) قيل: لا حاجة إلى هذا الكلام لأنَّ جميع الممكنات إذا كانت واقعة بتأثير قدرته فلو وقع واحد منها بقدرة العبد يلزم اجتماع علتين على معلول واحد بالشخص وأنه محال، كما بين في موضعه وليس بشيء، لأنَّ اللازم مما ذكرنا تأثير القدرة للعبد وقدرته تعالى في أفعاله، فيجوز أن يكون واقعة بمجموع القدرتين بأن يريد بكل منهما ما يريد الآخر، فحينئذ تكون العلة المستقلة مجموعها، وإن كان كل واحدة منهما كافية في وقوعه، كما الخشبة المحمولة لاثنين مع كون كل واحد منهما كافياً حملها، وهو مذهب الاستاذ أبي إسحاق في أفعال العبد فلا بد من اعتبار التمانع المشار إليه، بقوله : فلو أراد إلخ.
His saying: ('For if He willed ... etc.') He said: There is no need for this speech because if all possible things are produced by the influence of His power, then if one of them occurs by the power of the servant, it would necessitate two causes on one individual effect, and that is impossible, as is established in its place. And it is nothing, because what necessarily follows from what we mentioned is the influence of the power of the servant and His power, may He be exalted, in his acts; so it is possible that it occurs by the combination of the two powers, with each willing what the other wills; then the independent cause is their sum, even if each one of them alone is sufficient for its occurrence, like a plank carried by two [people] while each one of them alone is sufficient to carry it. And this is the doctrine of the master Abu Ishaq concerning the acts of the servant. So it is necessary to consider the mutual impediment indicated by his saying: 'For if He willed ... etc.'
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the argument that if God and the servant both exercise power over an act, then it must lead to impossibility. The objector says that the argument is unnecessary because if all possible things come from God, then if one act were produced by the servant, that would necessitate two causes (God and the servant) on one effect, which is impossible. The commentator responds that this objection is not valid because the implication we drew is that both powers influence the act, and it is possible that the act occurs by the combination of both powers, each willing the same thing, like two people carrying a plank where each alone is sufficient. This is the position of Abu Ishaq. Therefore, the argument for mutual impediment (tamānu') is still needed to show what happens when they will opposite things.

Key Terms
تمانع
علة
معلول
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument for mutual impediment (tamānu') is redundant because two causes on one effect is already impossible.
Correction: The commentator argues that two powers can cooperate (like two people carrying a plank), so the impossibility of two causes only holds when they will opposite things. Thus tamānu' is necessary to exclude cooperation.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why is the argument for mutual impediment (tamānu') necessary?
A) Because two causes on one effect is always impossible.
B) Because without assuming opposing wills, the two powers might cooperate, as in the example of two people carrying a plank.
C) Because the servant's power is nonexistent.
D) Because God's power alone is sufficient for all acts.

Key Takeaway
The argument for mutual impediment is necessary because it addresses the case when two powers will opposite things, not just when they cooperate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if your actions depend solely on your own strength, or do you acknowledge that every choice you make is only possible by the combination of your power and God’s will? How would your daily decisions change if you truly internalized this dependence?
?
When you see two forces pulling you in opposite directions (e.g., your desires vs. God's command), do you realize that this conflict can only be resolved by submitting to God's power? What is one area of your life where you are actively resisting God's will, and what would it cost you to yield?
?
If you truly believed that no good act happens without God's power and your effort combined, would you still take credit for your successes? What would it mean for your humility and gratitude to consistently attribute your achievements to this partnership?

One Act Between Two Agents: Abu'l-Husayn's View
Discusses the permissibility of one act between two agents with citations and objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether one act can be within the power of two agents and the significance of the term 'absolutely' in qualifying different positions.

Core Idea
One act can be attributed to two agents — but only under specific conditions.

This lesson delves into a nuanced theological question: can a single act be an object of power for two agents simultaneously? Abu'l-Husayn al-Basri's affirmative answer is examined, along with the Ash'ari qualification involving creator and acquirer. The precise meaning of 'absolutely' becomes crucial in understanding the sectarian divide.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the English translation to clarify meanings.
The Arabic is incomplete at the end; focus on the available content.
2
Identify the main positions
Note Abu'l-Husayn's absolute permissibility and the Ash'ari view distinguishing creator and acquirer.
3
Understand the objection and rebuttal
The objection claims Abu'l-Husayn didn't accept kasb; the rebuttal clarifies that 'absolutely' refers to his lack of detail, not to a statement about kasb.
4
Analyze al-Amidi's clarification
Al-Amidi's quote shows that 'absolutely' actually qualifies the impossibility for others, not Abu'l-Husayn's permissiveness.
5
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider how this discussion fits into debates about divine agency, causality, and the relationship between creator and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[المقصد الثاني : جواز مقدور بین قادرین ]
( هل يجوز مقدور بين قادرين؟ جوزه أبو الحسين البصري ) من المعتزلة ( مطلقاً) قيل : معناه من غير تفصيل بين أن يكون القادران مؤثرين أو كاسبين أو أحدهما مؤثراً والآخر كاسباً، ويرد عليه أنَّ أبا الحسين لم يقل بقدرة كاسبة بل هذا مذهب الأشاعرة،
قوله: (وتسمى تلك الصفة (قدرة باعتبار نسبتها إلى الطرفين واختياراً باعتبار تعلقها بأحدهما على وفق الإرادة. قوله : ( ويرد عليه إلخ ) هذا الإيراد مدفوع، لأن مراده بالإطلاق عدم التعرض لعدم التفصيل عنده، ولذا قال : من غير تفصيل، ولم يقل : سواء كان القدرتان موثرتين أو كاسبتين ومؤثرة وهو
قوله : (جوزه أبو الحسين مطلقاً نقل من الشارح أنَّ قيد الإطلاق هاهنا وقع في غير ،موقعه كما يدل عليه كلام الآمدي حيث قال : مذهب أصحابنا جواز مقدور بين قادرين خالق ومكتسب، وامتناع ذلك بين خالقين أو مكتسبين، وأجمعت المعتزلة على امتناع ذلك مطلقاً، غير أبي الحسين هذه عبارته، فالإطلاق فيها قيد للامتناع عند غير أبي الحسين لا للجواز عنده . قوله : ( ويرد عليه أن أبا الحسين إلخ) وحمل الإطلاق على مصطلح الأصول، وكون عدم
[The Second Aim: Permissibility of an Object of Power Between Two Powerful Agents] (Is an object of power between two powerful agents permissible? Abu'l-Husayn al-Basri allowed it) from the Mu'tazila (absolutely). It is said: Its meaning is without detail, whether the two powerful agents are effective or acquiring, or one effective and the other acquiring. And it is objected that Abu'l-Husayn did not speak of an acquiring power; rather, this is the school of the Ash'aris. His statement: (And that attribute is called power by virtue of its relation to the two sides, and choice by virtue of its attachment to one of them in accordance with will. His statement: (And it is objected etc.) This objection is repelled because his intention by 'absolutely' is not to address the lack of detail for him, and therefore he said 'without detail', and he did not say 'whether the two powers are effective or acquiring and effective', and it is. His statement: (Abu'l-Husayn allowed it absolutely) transmitted from the commentator that the qualification 'absolutely' here fell in its wrong place, as indicated by the statement of al-Amidi where he said: The school of our companions is the permissibility of an object of power between two powerful agents, a creator and an acquirer, and the impossibility of that between two creators or two acquirers. And the Mu'tazila are unanimous on the impossibility of that absolutely, except Abu'l-Husayn. This is his expression. So 'absolutely' here is a qualification for the impossibility for others than Abu'l-Husayn, not for the permissibility for him. His statement: (And it is objected that Abu'l-Husayn etc.) And the understanding of 'absolutely' according to the terminology of the principles, and that the non-
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage introduces the second mas'ala: whether one act can be within the power of two agents. It states that Abu'l-Husayn al-Basri (a Mu'tazili) allowed it absolutely. The gloss clarifies that 'absolutely' means without detailing whether the agents are effective or acquiring. An objection is raised that Abu'l-Husayn did not accept the concept of acquisition (kasb), which is an Ash'ari doctrine. The response in the gloss is that the objection is repelled because 'absolutely' refers to his own lack of detail, not to a comprehensive statement. Another gloss quotes al-Amidi's position: Ash'aris allow one act between a creator and an acquirer, but not between two creators or two acquirers. The Mu'tazila excepting Abu'l-Husayn unanimously forbid it. The term 'absolutely' actually qualifies the impossibility for others, not the permissibility for Abu'l-Husayn. The passage ends with a discussion of the term 'absolutely' in the context of principles (usul).

Key Terms
مقدور
قادر
كاسب
مؤثر
إطلاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'absolutely' (muttlaqan) in the phrase 'Abu'l-Husayn allowed it absolutely' is taken to mean that Abu'l-Husayn permitted it in all cases without exception.
Correction: Al-Amidi's quote clarifies that 'absolutely' actually qualifies the impossibility for other Mu'tazila, not Abu'l-Husayn's permissiveness. Abu'l-Husayn's allowance is without detail (tafsil), not necessarily without any condition.

Checkpoint
According to al-Amidi as quoted in the gloss, what is the Ash'ari position on an object of power between two agents?
A) It is allowed absolutely
B) It is allowed between a creator and an acquirer, but not between two creators or two acquirers
C) It is impossible between any two agents
D) It is allowed only between two creators

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'absolutely' in theological discussions can be ambiguous; careful reading of context is necessary to determine which position is being qualified.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that your actions are, according to the Ash'aris, created by God and acquired by you, do you truly feel the weight of responsibility for your sins, or do you sometimes slip into blaming God's decree? How can you train yourself to take full ownership of your choices without forgetting God's ultimate power?
?
The passage shows how a single word like 'absolutely' can be misinterpreted, leading to a misunderstanding of a scholar's position. Have you ever clung to a theological opinion without checking its exact wording in the original sources? How would it change your approach to learning if you admitted that precision matters more than allegiance to a group?
?
Abu'l-Husayn allowed one act between two agents absolutely, but the other scholars said it's only possible between a creator and an acquirer. In your own life, do you tend to see your good deeds as purely your own achievement or as a joint product of God's grace and your effort? Which view drives you to be more grateful and humble?

The Fourth Type: Act Within Power of Two Agents
Introduction to the permissibility and limitation of mutual hindrance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that an act can be within the power of two agents when one is creator and the other is acquirer, and how the mutual hindrance argument is limited to the case of multiple gods.

Core Idea
Not all dual agency leads to mutual hindrance; only when both have equal independent power.

This lesson introduces the key question: can a single act be within the power of two agents? The Ash'ari response is nuanced, rejecting absolute permissibility while affirming it between a creator and an acquirer. We begin by clarifying the meaning of absoluteness and the proper scope of the mutual hindrance argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Notice the structure: an objection based on mutual hindrance, then a clarification that it only applies to multiple gods.
The passage uses the term 'tamanu' (mutual hindrance) — a key concept in kalam.
2
Understand the limitation of mutual hindrance
The passage explains that the argument only holds when no preponderator exists, as in the case of multiple gods. For other cases, the stronger agent's will prevailing is not arbitrary.
3
Grasp the Ash'ari position
The Ash'aris permit an act being within the power of two agents specifically between a creator-power and an acquiring-power, not absolutely. This is based on the theory of kasb.
The servant's power does not create the act; it merely acquires it.

Source Text

النوع الرابع - المقصد الثاني : جواز مقدور بين قادرين
ومن يحذو حذوهم ويحتمل أن يقال : معنى الإطلاق بالنسبة إلى الخالق والمخلوق والمخلوقين وكأنه نظر إلى أن دليل التمانع إنما يتم إذا كان حصول مراد أحدهما دون الآخر ترجيحاً بلا مرجح كما في تعدد الآلهة، وأما في غيره فلا يتم، فإن الخالق أقدر من المخلوق، ويجوز أن يكون أحد المخلوقين أقدر من الآخر، فلا يكون وقوع مراد الأقدر تحكماً (و) جوزه ( الأصحاب ) لا مطلقاً، بل بين قادر خالق وقادر كاسب ( بناء على إثبات قدرة للعبد غير مؤثرة في مقدوره بل متعلقة به تعلق الكسب (مع شمول قدرة الله تعالى ) لجميع الأشياء، فيكون مقدور العبد كسباً مقدوراً لله تعالى الموافق لعبارة فإن جعل مبنى التفصيل القول بالقدرة الكلية وبمعنى إن كان المعتزلة مطلق قولهم بامتناع القدرة الغير المؤثرة. وكلام الآمدي في أبكار الأفكار حيث قال: مذهب أصحابنا جواز مقدور بين خالق وكاسب وامتناع ذلك بين خالقين وكاسبين واجتمعت المعتزلة على امتناع ذلك مطلقاً غير أبي الحسين، انتهى . فإنَّ معنى قوله : مطلقاً من غير تعرض للتفصيل لعدم القدرة الكاسبة عندهم لا العموم فمعنى قوله : غير أبي الحسي، أنه يجوز ذلك مطلقاً أي بدون التعرض للتفصيل لعدم القدرة الكاسبة عنده أيضاً. فما قيل إنه نقل عن الشارح أنه قيد الإطلاق في نقل مذهبه وقع موقعه كما يدل عليه كلام الأمدي حيث قال مذهب أصحابنا، فإنَّ الإطلاق منهما قيد للامتناع عند غير أبي الحسين لا الجواب عنده ليس بشيء، وقيل: في دفع الإيراد أن مراده التجويز مطلقاً على تقدير فرض القدرة الكاسبة، وفيه أنه حينئذ لا يكون منع المعتزلة وتجويزه على وتيرة واحدة، لأنَّ منعهم مبني على انتفاء القدرة الكاسبة وخلافه على فرضها، مع ان عبارة الآمدي وبيان المصنف يقتضي ذلك
قوله فلا يكون وقوع مراد الأقدر تحكماً ولا يلزم من ذلك أن لا يكون مقدور بين قادرين، لأن القدرة عند المعتزلة قبل الفعل بل يلزم تخلف أحد القدرتين لممانعة الأخرى .
التقييد والتفصيل لعدم الاحتياج بناء على نفيه القدرة الكاسبة بعيد إذا المتبادر منه هو الجواز في جميع الصور، وأما الجواب بأن أبا الحسين قال : ذلك على سبيل الفرض وتقرير حجة مذهب كما مر، نظير ذلك في إرادة الإرادة ففيه أنه لا يلائم خلافه لسائر المعتزلة في امتناع مقدور بين قدرتين كاسبتين، أو كاسبة ومؤثرة لأن مبنى كلامهم امتناع القدرة الكاسبة، كما صرح به المصنف وأبو الحسين قال بهذا المعنى، فتأمل.
الأشعري
قوله فلا يكون وقوع مراد الأقدر إلخ) فإن قلت: لا يجتمع حينئذ قدرتان مؤثرتان والكلام فيه . قلت أبو الحسين يقول بقبلية القدرة المؤثرة على الفعل، ومعنى المقدورية عنده أن القادر متمكن من إيجاده وتركه حتى لو تعلق إرادته بإيجاده، ولم توجد ممانعة الأقدر لأثر قدرته فيه بالفعل، فعلى هذا يوجد في الصورة المذكورة مقدور بين قادرين وإن لم يوجد موجود بین موجدين بالفعل.
The fourth type - the second objective: The permissibility of an act being within the power of two agents. And those who follow their way, and it may be said: the meaning of absoluteness with respect to the Creator and the creature and the creatures, and it is as if he considered that the argument of mutual hindrance only holds if the attainment of the will of one of them to the exclusion of the other is a preponderance without a preponderator as in the case of multiple gods, but in other than that it does not hold, for the Creator is more powerful than the creature, and it is permissible that one of the creatures be more powerful than the other, so the occurrence of the will of the more powerful is not arbitrary. And the companions permitted it, not absolutely, but between a creator-power and an acquiring-power, based on affirming a power for the servant that is not effective in its object but is related to it by the relation of acquisition, with the inclusion of the power of God Almighty over all things, so the servant's object of power is an acquisition that is also an object of power for God Almighty. This agrees with the expression: for he made the basis of the detail the statement of universal power, meaning that the Mu'tazila universally deny the non-effective power. And the statement of al-Amidi in Abkar al-Afkar where he said: The school of our companions is the permissibility of an act being within the power of a creator and an acquirer, and its impossibility between two creators and two acquirers, and the Mu'tazila agreed on its absolute impossibility except Abu al-Husayn, end. So the meaning of his statement 'absolutely' is without entering into detail, due to their denial of acquiring power, not generality. So the meaning of his statement 'except Abu al-Husayn' is that he permits it absolutely, i.e., without entering into detail, due to his denial of acquiring power as well. So what was said—that it was transmitted from the commentator that he restricted the absoluteness in transmitting his school—is correct, as indicated by the statement of al-Amidi where he said 'the school of our companions,' for the absoluteness from both of them is a restriction of impossibility for other than Abu al-Husayn, not the answer with him, is nothing. And it was said in repelling the objection: His intention is permission absolutely on the assumption of postulating an acquiring power. But there is in it that then the Mu'tazila's prohibition and his permission would not be on the same basis, because their prohibition is based on the absence of acquiring power, and his contrary is based on its assumption, while the wording of al-Amidi and the author's explanation require that. His statement: 'So the occurrence of the will of the more powerful is not arbitrary.' And it does not follow from that that there is no object of power between two agents, because power according to the Mu'tazila is before the act, but rather it necessitates the failure of one of the two powers due to the hindrance of the other. The restriction and detail due to lack of need, based on his denial of acquiring power, is far-fetched, since the apparent meaning is permission in all cases. As for the answer that Abu al-Husayn said that by way of hypothesis and establishing the proof of the school as mentioned, similar to that in the will of will, there is in it that it does not accord with his disagreement with the rest of the Mu'tazila in the impossibility of an object of power between two acquiring powers, or an acquiring and an effective power, because the basis of their statement is the impossibility of acquiring power, as the author explicitly stated, and Abu al-Husayn said this meaning, so reflect. Al-Ash'ari. His statement: 'So the occurrence of the will of the more powerful is not arbitrary' etc. For if you say: Then two effective powers do not coexist, and the discussion is about it. I say: Abu al-Husayn holds the priority of the effective power over the act, and the meaning of being an object of power for him is that the agent is capable of bringing it into existence and leaving it, so that if his will attaches to bringing it into existence, and there is no hindrance from the more powerful to the effect of his power on it in actuality, then on this basis there exists in the mentioned case an object of power between two agents, even if there does not exist an existent between two bringers-into-existence in actuality.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage discusses the second objective (al-maqsad al-thani) concerning the permissibility of a single act being within the power of two agents. It begins by explaining the meaning of absoluteness in the context of the Creator and creatures, noting that the argument of mutual hindrance (tamanu') applies only when the will of one is realized over the other without a preponderator, as in the case of multiple gods. In other cases, it does not apply because the Creator is more powerful, and one creature may be more powerful than another, so the stronger's will prevailing is not arbitrary. The Ash'ari school (al-ashab) permits it not absolutely, but specifically between a creator-power (qudra khaliqa) and an acquiring-power (qudra kasiba), based on affirming a power for the servant that is not effective (ghayr mu'aththira) but related by acquisition (kasb), while God's power encompasses all things. Thus, the servant's act is both an acquisition for him and an object of God's power. The passage then discusses al-Amidi's statement from Abkar al-Afkar, summarizing the Ash'ari position: permissibility between a creator and an acquirer, impossibility between two creators or two acquirers, and the Mu'tazila's absolute denial except Abu al-Husayn. The text clarifies that 'absolutely' here means without entering into detail, due to their denial of acquiring power, not generality. It then addresses objections: some said the commentator restricted absoluteness, but al-Amidi's wording shows it is a restriction of impossibility for others, not of Abu al-Husayn's response. Another objection is that Abu al-Husayn's permission is hypothetical, but this conflicts with his disagreement with other Mu'tazila. The passage ends with a gloss explaining Abu al-Husayn's view: effective power is prior to the act, and an act is an object of power if the agent can produce or leave it, even if a stronger agent could hinder it. Thus, an act can be an object of two powers potentially, even if not actualized by both.

Key Terms
مقدور بين قادرين
تمَانع
قدرة خالقة
قدرة كاسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if two agents have power over the same act, they must conflict mutually.
Correction: The Ash'aris show that conflict only occurs when both are equally independent and no preponderator exists. When one is creator and the other acquirer, the act can be an object of both without conflict.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does the argument of mutual hindrance (tamanu') apply?
A) When two agents have power over the same act in all circumstances
B) Only when the two agents are equal and independent, like multiple gods
C) Only when one agent is a creator and the other an acquirer
D) When the stronger agent's will prevails

Key Takeaway
An act can be within the power of two agents without mutual hindrance if one has a creator-power and the other an acquiring-power, as per the Ash'ari theory of kasb.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you attribute it entirely to your own choice, or do you secretly feel that God's power overrode your will? How does this lesson's distinction between creator-power and acquiring-power help you see your accountability more clearly? What will you change in how you think about sin tonight?
?
Think of a recent success. Did you fully thank God while acknowledging your own effort as mere acquisition? Are you comfortable with your role being non-creative yet real? If not, what does that reveal about your heart's attachment to self-sufficiency?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that human power would compete with God's. Do you ever feel like your will is in competition with God's will? How does this passage's explanation of kasb eliminate that feeling? Will you now trust that your efforts are accepted by God as acquisitions, even though He alone creates the outcome?

Causality and Created Things

Al-Amidi's Statement on the Ash'ari and Mu'tazila Positions
Al-Amidi's summary of positions and clarification of 'absolutely'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Amidi's summary of the Ash'ari and Mu'tazila positions on the permissibility of an act being within the power of two agents, including the exception of Abu al-Husayn.

Core Idea
Al-Amidi clarifies that 'absolutely' in Mu'tazila denial means without detail, not universally.

This lesson centers on al-Amidi's concise statement from Abkar al-Afkar, which neatly summarizes the schools' positions. The passage also clarifies a key ambiguity: what the Mu'tazila mean by 'absolutely' (mutlaqan) and why Abu al-Husayn is an exception.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Amidi's statement
Read the Arabic passage containing al-Amidi's quote. Note the three cases: permissibility between creator and acquirer, impossibility between two creators or two acquirers, and the Mu'tazila absolute denial except Abu al-Husayn.
2
Understand the meaning of 'absolutely'
The author explains that 'absolutely' here means the Mu'tazila deny without entering into detail, due to their denial of acquiring power. It does not mean universally.
This clarification resolves a potential misunderstanding.
3
Examine Abu al-Husayn's exception
Abu al-Husayn permits it absolutely even without the framework of acquiring power, but this is because he also denies acquiring power, so his permission is on a different basis.

Commentary

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'absolutely' (mutlaqan) mean in the Mu'tazila statement of impossibility?
A) Universal and unconditional denial
B) Without entering into detail, due to their denial of acquiring power
C) Only between two creators
D) Only between two acquirers

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own understanding of Islamic theology, are there doctrines you accept 'absolutely' without delving into the nuances? How might this lesson's clarification of the Mu'tazila's 'without detail' challenge you to re-examine your own assumptions?
?
The Mu'tazila rejected acquiring power because they saw it as an unnecessary compromise. Have you ever rejected a concept in your faith because it seemed to complicate your understanding, without taking the time to explore its depth? What would it take for you to move from a blanket rejection to a nuanced understanding?
?
The commentator defended the restriction of 'absolutely' to mean specifically 'without detail' for the Mu'tazila. This shows how a single word can carry great weight in theological discourse. Are you careful with the words you use in matters of faith, or do you sometimes speak in absolutes without considering the distinctions? Commit to being more precise in your religious language starting today.

Objections and the Resolution on Effective Power
Refutation of objections regarding coexistence of two effective powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections raised against the Ash'ari position and how Abu al-Husayn's view of effective power as prior to the act resolves the issue of two agents having power over the same act.

Core Idea
Two effective powers cannot coexist on one act, but a potential power and an actual power can.

This lesson examines objections to the Ash'ari position, particularly the claim that if both agents have effective power, two effective powers would coexist. The response relies on Abu al-Husayn's nuanced view that effective power is prior to the act, so the act can be an object of power for two agents potentially, even if not actually produced by both.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage that includes the objection: 'Then two effective powers do not coexist' and the response that Abu al-Husayn holds power is prior to the act.
2
Understand the concept of priority of power
Abu al-Husayn's view: power (qudra) exists before the act, and the agent is capable of producing or leaving it. This allows the act to be an object of power for two agents potentially.
This is distinct from saying both produce the act simultaneously.
3
See the resolution
Thus, there can be an object of power between two agents (mashy' bayn qadrayn) even if there is no existing act produced by both (mawjud bayn mawjidayn).
This upholds the Ash'ari position while avoiding absurdity.

قوله فلا يكون وقوع مراد الأقدر تحكماً ولا يلزم من ذلك أن لا يكون مقدور بين قادرين، لأن القدرة عند المعتزلة قبل الفعل بل يلزم تخلف أحد القدرتين لممانعة الأخرى .
التقييد والتفصيل لعدم الاحتياج بناء على نفيه القدرة الكاسبة بعيد إذا المتبادر منه هو الجواز في جميع الصور، وأما الجواب بأن أبا الحسين قال : ذلك على سبيل الفرض وتقرير حجة مذهب كما مر، نظير ذلك في إرادة الإرادة ففيه أنه لا يلائم خلافه لسائر المعتزلة في امتناع مقدور بين قدرتين كاسبتين، أو كاسبة ومؤثرة لأن مبنى كلامهم امتناع القدرة الكاسبة، كما صرح به المصنف وأبو الحسين قال بهذا المعنى، فتأمل.

The fourth type - the second objective: The permissibility of an act being within the power of two agents. And those who follow their way, and it may be said: the meaning of absoluteness with respect to the Creator and the creature and the creatures, and it is as if he considered that the argument of mutual hindrance only holds if the attainment of the will of one of them to the exclusion of the other is a preponderance without a preponderator as in the case of multiple gods, but in other than that it does not hold, for the Creator is more powerful than the creature, and it is permissible that one of the creatures be more powerful than the other, so the occurrence of the will of the more powerful is not arbitrary. And the companions permitted it, not absolutely, but between a creator-power and an acquiring-power, based on affirming a power for the servant that is not effective in its object but is related to it by the relation of acquisition, with the inclusion of the power of God Almighty over all things, so the servant's object of power is an acquisition that is also an object of power for God Almighty. This agrees with the expression: for he made the basis of the detail the statement of universal power, meaning that the Mu'tazila universally deny the non-effective power. And the statement of al-Amidi in Abkar al-Afkar where he said: The school of our companions is the permissibility of an act being within the power of a creator and an acquirer, and its impossibility between two creators and two acquirers, and the Mu'tazila agreed on its absolute impossibility except Abu al-Husayn, end. So the meaning of his statement 'absolutely' is without entering into detail, due to their denial of acquiring power, not generality. So the meaning of his statement 'except Abu al-Husayn' is that he permits it absolutely, i.e., without entering into detail, due to his denial of acquiring power as well. So what was said—that it was transmitted from the commentator that he restricted the absoluteness in transmitting his school—is correct, as indicated by the statement of al-Amidi where he said 'the school of our companions,' for the absoluteness from both of them is a restriction of impossibility for other than Abu al-Husayn, not the answer with him, is nothing. And it was said in repelling the objection: His intention is permission absolutely on the assumption of postulating an acquiring power. But there is in it that then the Mu'tazila's prohibition and his permission would not be on the same basis, because their prohibition is based on the absence of acquiring power, and his contrary is based on its assumption, while the wording of al-Amidi and the author's explanation require that. His statement: 'So the occurrence of the will of the more powerful is not arbitrary.' And it does not follow from that that there is no object of power between two agents, because power according to the Mu'tazila is before the act, but rather it necessitates the failure of one of the two powers due to the hindrance of the other. The restriction and detail due to lack of need, based on his denial of acquiring power, is far-fetched, since the apparent meaning is permission in all cases. As for the answer that Abu al-Husayn said that by way of hypothesis and establishing the proof of the school as mentioned, similar to that in the will of will, there is in it that it does not accord with his disagreement with the rest of the Mu'tazila in the impossibility of an object of power between two acquiring powers, or an acquiring and an effective power, because the basis of their statement is the impossibility of acquiring power, as the author explicitly stated, and Abu al-Husayn said this meaning, so reflect. Al-Ash'ari. His statement: 'So the occurrence of the will of the more powerful is not arbitrary' etc. For if you say: Then two effective powers do not coexist, and the discussion is about it. I say: Abu al-Husayn holds the priority of the effective power over the act, and the meaning of being an object of power for him is that the agent is capable of bringing it into existence and leaving it, so that if his will attaches to bringing it into existence, and there is no hindrance from the more powerful to the effect of his power on it in actuality, then on this basis there exists in the mentioned case an object of power between two agents, even if there does not exist an existent between two bringers-into-existence in actuality.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage discusses the second objective (al-maqsad al-thani) concerning the permissibility of a single act being within the power of two agents. It begins by explaining the meaning of absoluteness in the context of the Creator and creatures, noting that the argument of mutual hindrance (tamanu') applies only when the will of one is realized over the other without a preponderator, as in the case of multiple gods. In other cases, it does not apply because the Creator is more powerful, and one creature may be more powerful than another, so the stronger's will prevailing is not arbitrary. The Ash'ari school (al-ashab) permits it not absolutely, but specifically between a creator-power (qudra khaliqa) and an acquiring-power (qudra kasiba), based on affirming a power for the servant that is not effective (ghayr mu'aththira) but related by acquisition (kasb), while God's power encompasses all things. Thus, the servant's act is both an acquisition for him and an object of God's power. The passage then discusses al-Amidi's statement from Abkar al-Afkar, summarizing the Ash'ari position: permissibility between a creator and an acquirer, impossibility between two creators or two acquirers, and the Mu'tazila's absolute denial except Abu al-Husayn. The text clarifies that 'absolutely' here means without entering into detail, due to their denial of acquiring power, not generality. It then addresses objections: some said the commentator restricted absoluteness, but al-Amidi's wording shows it is a restriction of impossibility for others, not of Abu al-Husayn's response. Another objection is that Abu al-Husayn's permission is hypothetical, but this conflicts with his disagreement with other Mu'tazila. The passage ends with a gloss explaining Abu al-Husayn's view: effective power is prior to the act, and an act is an object of power if the agent can produce or leave it, even if a stronger agent could hinder it. Thus, an act can be an object of two powers potentially, even if not actualized by both.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an act is within the power of two agents, both must actually bring it into existence, leading to two effective powers coexisting.
Correction: Abu al-Husayn explains that effective power is prior to the act, so an act can be an object of power for two agents potentially without both producing it.

Checkpoint
According to Abu al-Husayn as presented in the passage, how can an act be an object of power for two agents?
A) Both agents bring the act into existence simultaneously
B) The act is a potential object of power for both, but only one may actualize it
C) The weaker agent's power is negated by the stronger
D) The act is divided between the two agents

Key Takeaway
The objection of two effective powers is resolved by Abu al-Husayn's view that power precedes the act, allowing an act to be an object of power for two agents potentially without actual dual production.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action, do you ever feel that your power and God's power are in conflict? How does this lesson's distinction between potential and actual power reshape your understanding of your own responsibility?
?
In moments of failure or sin, do you tend to attribute the act entirely to yourself or entirely to God? How can the concept that an act can be an object of power for both you and God without dual production help you avoid extremes of pride or despair?
?
Reflect on a time when you felt your will was overwhelmed by circumstances. Did you doubt that you had any effective power? How does the idea that power precedes the act and is about capability rather than actuality affect your view of your own agency?

Causality and Created Things

Mu'tazila's Rejection and Ash'ari Agreement on Effective Powers
Presents the Mu'tazila's rejection and the Ash'ari concession on effective powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila deny any act falling under two powers and why the Ash'aris agree that two effective powers cannot coexist on one act.

Core Idea
Power must be effective; two effective powers on one act cause mutual hindrance.

The Mu'tazila build their theology on the principle that power is essentially effective. From this, they conclude that no act can be subject to two powers, because they would hinder each other. The Ash'aris, despite their different framework, concede this point for effective powers, leaving their distinctive position for acquiring powers.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage once to grasp the flow of the argument. Focus on the Mu'tazila's assertion and the Ash'ari conditional agreement.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila's premise
Notice that the Mu'tazila base their denial on the impossibility of a non-effective power. For them, power is always effective (mu'aththira).
This premise is key to their entire theory of causality.
3
Identify the Ash'ari agreement
The Ash'aris who permit two powers generally draw a distinction, but they agree with the Mu'tazila that two effective powers are impossible. See the word 'ittafaqū' (they agreed).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ومنعه المعتزلة) أي منعوا جواز كون مقدور بين قادرين مطلقاً (بناء على امتناع قدرة غير مؤثرة على رأيهم بل لا تكون القدرة عندهم إلا مؤثرة (فيلزم التمانع على تقدير كون مقدور بين قادرين ) والمجوزون من أصحابنا) لكون مقدور بين قدرة كاسبة وقدرة مؤثرة كما مر (اتفقوا على امتناع) مقدور بين قدرتين مؤثرتين للتمانع ( و ) على امتناع مقدور بين (قدرتين كاسبتين، لأن الكسب أن يخلق الله تعالى فعلاً متعلقاً للقدرة الحادثة، وأنها ) أي القدرة الحادثة (لا تتعلق بفعل خارج عن المحل أي محل تلك القدرة الحادثة ( فلا يقدر زيد على فعل عمرو، ولا يتصور اثنان هما محل لفعل واحد ) بل يكون كل واحد من الاثنين محلاً لفعل مغاير، ولو بالشخص لفعل الآخر، فلا يمكن اجتماع قدرتين كاسبتين على فعل واحد شخصي .
قوله: (ومنعه المعتزلة) أي كلهم غير أبي الحسين كما نقله الآمدي، وهذه المسألة أعني جواز اجتماع القدرتين وعدمه غير المسألة التي تجيء في الإلهيات لأن قدرته تعالى شاملة لجميع الممكنات خلافاً للجبائية، فإنهم قالوا إنه تعالى لا يقدر على غير مقدور العبد، فما قيل : إن المانعين هم الجبائية القائلون بأن الله تعالى لا يقدر على نفس مقدور العبد، وهم . قوله : ( محلاً لفعل أي كل واحد منها محصل لفعل. قوله : ( يتأتى السبب عند الأشاعرة عادية، وعند المعتزلة حقيقية، فهذا التعريف متفق عليه قوله : ( ومنعه المعتزلة) الظاهر أن المانع بعضهم وهم الجبائية القائلون بأن الله تعالى لا يقدر على نفس مقدور العبد تعالى عن ذلك علواً كبيراً، ففيما ذكر في أبكار الأفكار من إجماع المعتزلة على ذلك سوى أبي الحسين تأمل. قوله : (لأن الكسب هو أن يخلق الله تعالى فيه مسامحة والمقصود أن الكسب حالة يقارنها الخلق . قوله: (بل يكون كل واحد من الاثنين إلخ) هذا بناء على ما سيحقق من أن الاجتماع بين الشيئين اثنان وأما على ما ذهب إليه بعض عظماء الصناعة الكلامية من أن الاجتماع الواحد قائم
And the Mu'tazila denied it, i.e., they absolutely denied the permissibility of an act being within the power of two agents, based on the impossibility of a non-effective power in their view; rather, power for them is only effective. So mutual hindrance follows on the assumption of an act being within the power of two agents. And those who permit it among our companions, for an act being within the power of an acquiring power and an effective power as mentioned, agreed on the impossibility of an act being within the power of two effective powers due to mutual hindrance, and on the impossibility of an act being within the power of two acquiring powers, because acquisition (kasb) is that God creates an act related to the originated power, and that it—i.e., the originated power—does not relate to an act outside the locus, i.e., the locus of that originated power. So Zayd cannot have power over 'Amr's act, nor can two be imagined as the locus for one act. Rather, each of the two is a locus for a different act, even if identical in kind to the other's act, so it is impossible for two acquiring powers to come together upon one individual act. His statement: 'And the Mu'tazila denied it' i.e., all of them except Abu al-Husayn, as al-Amidi transmitted. And this issue, I mean the permissibility and impermissibility of the conjunction of two powers, is different from the issue that comes in the Divine Matters, because His power encompasses all possible things, contrary to the Jubba'iyya, for they said that He is not able over other than the object of the servant's power. So what was said—that the deniers are the Jubba'iyya who say that God is not able over the very object of the servant's power, and they are... His statement: 'a locus for an act' i.e., each one of them is the locus of an act. His statement: 'The cause (sabab) according to the Ash'aris is customary, and according to the Mu'tazila is real, so this definition is agreed upon.' His statement: 'And the Mu'tazila denied it' the apparent meaning is that the deniers are some of them, namely the Jubba'iyya who say that God is not able over the very object of the servant's power, exalted is He above that greatly. So what is mentioned in Abkar al-Afkar of the consensus of the Mu'tazila on that except Abu al-Husayn is questionable. His statement: 'Because acquisition is that God creates...' is a leniency, and the intended meaning is that acquisition is a state accompanied by creation. His statement: 'Rather, each of the two is a locus...' is based on what will be verified: that the conjunction of two things is two, whereas according to some great masters of the science of kalam, the single conjunction is subsistent.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the second objective, presenting the Mu'tazila's absolute rejection of any act being within the power of two agents, because they hold that power must be effective (mu'aththira); a non-effective power is impossible. Therefore, if two powers were directed at one act, mutual hindrance (tamanu') would result. The Ash'aris who permit it—between an acquiring power and an effective power—agree on the impossibility of two effective powers and also on the impossibility of two acquiring powers. The reason given for the impossibility of two acquiring powers is that acquisition (kasb) means God creates an act that is related to the originated power, but that originated power cannot relate to an act outside its locus. Consequently, one person cannot have power over another's act, and two persons cannot be the locus for a single individual act; rather, each is the locus for a distinct act, even if identical in kind. Thus, two acquiring powers cannot coincide on one individual act. The passage then provides glosses. First, it clarifies that the Mu'tazila who deny are all except Abu al-Husayn, as al-Amidi reported. It distinguishes this issue from the one in Ilahiyyat concerning God's power encompassing all possibles, contrary to the Jubba'iyya who say God cannot will what the servant wills. A gloss notes that the apparent deniers are the Jubba'iyya, and al-Amidi's claim of consensus except Abu al-Husayn is questionable. Another gloss explains that 'locus for an act' means each agent is the locus of an act. Another mentions the definition of cause (sabab) as customary for Ash'aris and real for Mu'tazila. Finally, a gloss on 'rather each of the two is a locus' explains that it is based on the view that the conjunction of two things is two, not a single conjunction.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila and Ash'aris agree that two effective powers cannot share one act due to mutual hindrance.

Causality and Created Things

Ash'ari Impossibility of Two Acquiring Powers
Explains the Ash'ari argument against two acquiring powers on one act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'aris hold that two acquiring powers cannot relate to one individual act, based on the nature of kasb and the locus restriction.

Core Idea
Each acquiring power is confined to its own locus, making a shared act impossible.

Having conceded the impossibility of two effective powers, the Ash'aris now explain their unique position on acquiring powers (qudrat kasibiyya). They argue that acquisition (kasb) entails a specific relationship between the power and an act in its own locus, so two acquiring powers cannot direct the same individual act.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on acquiring powers
Read from the point where the Ash'aris discuss the impossibility of two acquiring powers. Pay attention to the definition of kasb.
2
Understand the definition of kasb
Kasb is defined as God creating an act related to an originated power. Note that the originated power does not relate to an act outside its locus.
This definition is crucial for understanding the restriction.
3
Follow the implication
Since each power is tied to its own locus, no one can have power over another's act, and two loci cannot be the site of one identical act.

(ومنعه المعتزلة) أي منعوا جواز كون مقدور بين قادرين مطلقاً (بناء على امتناع قدرة غير مؤثرة على رأيهم بل لا تكون القدرة عندهم إلا مؤثرة (فيلزم التمانع على تقدير كون مقدور بين قادرين ) والمجوزون من أصحابنا) لكون مقدور بين قدرة كاسبة وقدرة مؤثرة كما مر (اتفقوا على امتناع) مقدور بين قدرتين مؤثرتين للتمانع ( و ) على امتناع مقدور بين (قدرتين كاسبتين، لأن الكسب أن يخلق الله تعالى فعلاً متعلقاً للقدرة الحادثة، وأنها ) أي القدرة الحادثة (لا تتعلق بفعل خارج عن المحل أي محل تلك القدرة الحادثة ( فلا يقدر زيد على فعل عمرو، ولا يتصور اثنان هما محل لفعل واحد ) بل يكون كل واحد من الاثنين محلاً لفعل مغاير، ولو بالشخص لفعل الآخر، فلا يمكن اجتماع قدرتين كاسبتين على فعل واحد شخصي .
قوله: (ومنعه المعتزلة) أي كلهم غير أبي الحسين كما نقله الآمدي، وهذه المسألة أعني جواز اجتماع القدرتين وعدمه غير المسألة التي تجيء في الإلهيات لأن قدرته تعالى شاملة لجميع الممكنات خلافاً للجبائية، فإنهم قالوا إنه تعالى لا يقدر على غير مقدور العبد، فما قيل : إن المانعين هم الجبائية القائلون بأن الله تعالى لا يقدر على نفس مقدور العبد، وهم . قوله : ( محلاً لفعل أي كل واحد منها محصل لفعل. قوله : ( يتأتى السبب عند الأشاعرة عادية، وعند المعتزلة حقيقية، فهذا التعريف متفق عليه قوله : ( ومنعه المعتزلة) الظاهر أن المانع بعضهم وهم الجبائية القائلون بأن الله تعالى لا يقدر على نفس مقدور العبد تعالى عن ذلك علواً كبيراً، ففيما ذكر في أبكار الأفكار من إجماع المعتزلة على ذلك سوى أبي الحسين تأمل. قوله : (لأن الكسب هو أن يخلق الله تعالى فيه مسامحة والمقصود أن الكسب حالة يقارنها الخلق . قوله: (بل يكون كل واحد من الاثنين إلخ) هذا بناء على ما سيحقق من أن الاجتماع بين الشيئين اثنان وأما على ما ذهب إليه بعض عظماء الصناعة الكلامية من أن الاجتماع الواحد قائم
And the Mu'tazila denied it, i.e., they absolutely denied the permissibility of an act being within the power of two agents, based on the impossibility of a non-effective power in their view; rather, power for them is only effective. So mutual hindrance follows on the assumption of an act being within the power of two agents. And those who permit it among our companions, for an act being within the power of an acquiring power and an effective power as mentioned, agreed on the impossibility of an act being within the power of two effective powers due to mutual hindrance, and on the impossibility of an act being within the power of two acquiring powers, because acquisition (kasb) is that God creates an act related to the originated power, and that it—i.e., the originated power—does not relate to an act outside the locus, i.e., the locus of that originated power. So Zayd cannot have power over 'Amr's act, nor can two be imagined as the locus for one act. Rather, each of the two is a locus for a different act, even if identical in kind to the other's act, so it is impossible for two acquiring powers to come together upon one individual act. His statement: 'And the Mu'tazila denied it' i.e., all of them except Abu al-Husayn, as al-Amidi transmitted. And this issue, I mean the permissibility and impermissibility of the conjunction of two powers, is different from the issue that comes in the Divine Matters, because His power encompasses all possible things, contrary to the Jubba'iyya, for they said that He is not able over other than the object of the servant's power. So what was said—that the deniers are the Jubba'iyya who say that God is not able over the very object of the servant's power, and they are... His statement: 'a locus for an act' i.e., each one of them is the locus of an act. His statement: 'The cause (sabab) according to the Ash'aris is customary, and according to the Mu'tazila is real, so this definition is agreed upon.' His statement: 'And the Mu'tazila denied it' the apparent meaning is that the deniers are some of them, namely the Jubba'iyya who say that God is not able over the very object of the servant's power, exalted is He above that greatly. So what is mentioned in Abkar al-Afkar of the consensus of the Mu'tazila on that except Abu al-Husayn is questionable. His statement: 'Because acquisition is that God creates...' is a leniency, and the intended meaning is that acquisition is a state accompanied by creation. His statement: 'Rather, each of the two is a locus...' is based on what will be verified: that the conjunction of two things is two, whereas according to some great masters of the science of kalam, the single conjunction is subsistent.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the second objective, presenting the Mu'tazila's absolute rejection of any act being within the power of two agents, because they hold that power must be effective (mu'aththira); a non-effective power is impossible. Therefore, if two powers were directed at one act, mutual hindrance (tamanu') would result. The Ash'aris who permit it—between an acquiring power and an effective power—agree on the impossibility of two effective powers and also on the impossibility of two acquiring powers. The reason given for the impossibility of two acquiring powers is that acquisition (kasb) means God creates an act that is related to the originated power, but that originated power cannot relate to an act outside its locus. Consequently, one person cannot have power over another's act, and two persons cannot be the locus for a single individual act; rather, each is the locus for a distinct act, even if identical in kind. Thus, two acquiring powers cannot coincide on one individual act. The passage then provides glosses. First, it clarifies that the Mu'tazila who deny are all except Abu al-Husayn, as al-Amidi reported. It distinguishes this issue from the one in Ilahiyyat concerning God's power encompassing all possibles, contrary to the Jubba'iyya who say God cannot will what the servant wills. A gloss notes that the apparent deniers are the Jubba'iyya, and al-Amidi's claim of consensus except Abu al-Husayn is questionable. Another gloss explains that 'locus for an act' means each agent is the locus of an act. Another mentions the definition of cause (sabab) as customary for Ash'aris and real for Mu'tazila. Finally, a gloss on 'rather each of the two is a locus' explains that it is based on the view that the conjunction of two things is two, not a single conjunction.

كسب
محل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that two acquiring powers could direct the same act since they are not effective, but the locus restriction prevents it.
Correction: Each acquiring power is inherently tied to its own locus, so a single act cannot have two loci.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can Zayd not have power over 'Amr's act?
A) Because God's power overrides
B) Because the originated power does not relate to acts outside its own locus
C) Because 'Amr's act is too difficult
D) Because the Mu'tazila forbid it

Key Takeaway
Two acquiring powers cannot share one act because each power is restricted to its own locus, and an act cannot have two loci.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action, do you ever pause to consider that your power is limited to your own self, and that no one else can truly share in your acts? How does this realization affect your sense of personal responsibility before Allah?
?
The Ash'ari argument emphasizes that each act has a single, unique locus. In your daily life, where might you be tempted to believe that multiple powers (people) can jointly cause a single outcome, and how does this lesson redirect your understanding of divine causality?
?
Since your acquiring power is confined to your own locus, what does that mean for your reliance on others vs. reliance on Allah? Are you sometimes attributing shared causality to created beings when only Allah can truly cause?

Proof that Originated Power Must Be Simultaneous with the Act
Al-Jurjani presents the proof that originated power must be concurrent with the act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that originated power must exist concurrently with the act it enables.

Core Idea
Originated power cannot precede or follow its act — it must accompany it.

Al-Jurjani presents a rigorous proof that the created power (qudra ḥāditha) by which human actions are performed cannot exist before the act, nor after it, but must be simultaneous. This lesson walks through the argument step by step, showing why any prior existence leads to contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation to get the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the structure: the proof is followed by clarifying comments.
2
Understand the proof's premise
The proof starts with the supposition that if power precedes the act, then the act would have to be possible at that earlier time.
But is the act possible before its occurrence?
3
Identify the contradiction
If the act were possible before, it would have to be both prior and simultaneous (since it would be possible before but actual later) — a contradiction.
The possible cannot imply an impossibility.
4
Follow the conclusion
Since the act is not possible before, power cannot exist then. It also cannot exist after. Therefore it must be simultaneous.
This establishes the necessary simultaneity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع - المقصد الخامس : القدرة الحادثة وتعلقها بالفعل
لأن كون المتقدم على الفعل مقارناً له يستلزم اجتماع النقيضين، أعني كونه متقدماً وغير متقدم فقد لزم من وجود الفعل قبله محال فلا يكون ممكناً إذ الممكن لا يستلزم المستحيل بالذات، وإذا لم يكن الفعل ممكناً قبله لم يكن مقدورا قبله، فلا تكون القدرة عليه موجودة حينئذ ، ولا شك أن وجود القدرة بعد الفعل، مما لا فتعين أن تكون موجودة معه وهو المطلوب
قوله : ( وإذا لم يكن الفعل ممكناً (قبله أي بالنسبة إلى القدرة الحادثة لم يكن مقدوراً لها
قوله : ( فلا تكون القدرة عليه أي على الفعل موجودة إذ وجود القدرة في زمان لا مقدور فيه أصلاً محال وإن كان وجودها بدون المقدور ممكناً، بل متحققاً كما في القدرة القديمة لأن القدرة صفة بها يتمكن الفعل والترك فلو لم يكن مما يمكن عليه لم تكن القدرة متحققة.
Fourth type - Fifth aim: The originated power and its relation to the act. Because the fact that what is prior to the act is simultaneous with it necessitates the conjunction of two contradictories, namely its being prior and not prior. So the existence of the act before it entails an impossibility, so it is not possible, since the possible does not entail an impossibility by itself. And if the act is not possible before it, it is not within power before it. So power over it would not exist then. And there is no doubt that the existence of power after the act is not [possible]; so it must be existing with it, and that is what is required. His saying: (And if the act is not possible before it) i.e. in relation to the originated power, it is not within its power. His saying: (Then power over it is not existing) i.e. over the act, because existence of power in a time in which there is no object of power at all is impossible, although existence of power without the object of power is possible, indeed actual, as in the eternal power. For power is an attribute by which one is able to do and leave, so if there were nothing which it could be able over, power would not be realized.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents the proof that originated power (qudra ḥāditha) must be concurrent with the act (fiʿl). The passage begins by stating the type and aim of the discussion. The proof is as follows: If the power were prior to the act, then the act would have to be possible at that prior time. But the act is impossible before its time, because that would require the act to exist before its time, which is a contradiction (the act would be both prior and not prior). Since the possible does not entail an intrinsic impossibility, the act cannot be possible before the power. Consequently, it cannot be within power (maqdūr) before that time. Therefore, power over the act cannot exist at that prior time. It also cannot exist after the act, so it must exist simultaneously with the act. The commentator then explains the phrases: 'if the act is not possible before it' means in relation to the originated power, it is not an object of power. 'Then power over it is not existing' means that existence of power in a time without any object of power is impossible, though existence of power without an object is possible (as in eternal power), but power is an attribute that requires a possible object; otherwise it would not be realized.

Key Terms
القدرة الحادثة
المقدور
المستحيل بالذات
اجتماع النقيضين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that originated power can exist at a time when there is no possible act (object of power), similar to divine power which exists without an object.
Correction: The passage clarifies that while eternal power can exist without an object, originated power is an attribute that requires a possible object at the same moment; otherwise it would not be realized as power.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what happens if we suppose originated power exists before the act?
A) The act becomes impossible.
B) The act would be possible before its time, leading to a contradiction.
C) Power would become eternal.
D) The act would be caused by something else.

Key Takeaway
Originated power must exist simultaneously with its act; it cannot precede or follow it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Consider a sin you intend to leave but keep postponing. Your power to leave it exists only when you actually leave it. Are you relying on a future power that may not come? What specific step can you take today to break that cycle?
?
This lesson emphasizes that power without an object is impossible for created beings. Look at your day so far: have you used your current power for acts pleasing to Allah, or have you let it waste away in idleness? What is one immediate act of obedience you can perform within the next hour?
