Objection and Answer: Power and Bringing About the Act
Al-Jurjani presents and refutes an objection about power and iqa'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that power can precede the act by being related to its 'bringing about' (iqa') and the refutation that this either collapses into the act or causes an infinite regress.

Core Idea
The act's 'bringing about' either is the act itself or leads to an endless chain.

Al-Jurjani presents a subtle objection: perhaps power in the present moment is connected not to the act itself but to its bringing about at a later time. This would allow power to exist before the act. The refutation examines what 'bringing about' really means, and shows the difficulty. Let's follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening lines of the passage where the objector suggests power can be prior if it is connected to iqa' (bringing about) rather than to the act directly.
The objector says power exists in the earlier moment but its connection is to the act in the next moment.
2
Identify the two connections of power
Notice the distinction: a meaningful connection (ta'alluq ma'nawi) that cannot be delayed, and a productive connection that can be delayed. The objection concerns the productive one.
The meaningful connection is about capability; the productive connection is about causing existence.
3
Analyze the refutation
The refutation splits iqa' into two cases: if iqa' is exactly the act's existence, it's impossible earlier; if it's something else, it itself needs an effect of power, leading to infinite regress.
The objection that iqa' is a mere mental consideration is also addressed but text cuts off.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فإن قيل إلخ) منع لقوله فلا تكون القدرة عليه موجودة فيه أنه يجوز أن تكون القدرة في الزمان السابق على وجود المقدور موجودة مع عدم امكان الفعل في ذلك الحال بناء، على أن يكون تعلقها في ثاني الحال وامكان المقدور ، إنما يستلزم التعلق لأنها حال وجود الفعل لا حال وجود القدرة ، فلا يلزم ما ذكر من المحال من كون القدرة متقدمة وغير متقدمة هذا على طبق ما قرره الشارح، ويرد عليه أن التعرض للايقاع حينئذ مستدرك إذ يكفي أن يقال : إن القدرة على حصول الفعل في ثاني الحال وهو لا يستدعي امكانه في الحال إلى آخر. وإن للقدرة تعلقين تعلق معنوي يقال : فلان قادر عليه أي متمكن من فعله وتركه وبه يميز المقدور بالنسبة إلى القادر وهذا لا يمكن تأخيره عن القدرة وهو المراد من قولنا فيما سبق انتفاء المقدور يستلزم انتفاء القدرة، وسيجيء في كلام الشارح من أن وجود القدرة بدون هذا التعلق مما يأباه البديهة وتعلق يترتب عليه الوجود وهو متأخر عن تعلق الإرادة، ويجوز تأخره عن وجود القدرة والكلام في الأول دون الثاني، وتقرير هذا الاعتراض عندي إن القدرة السابقة، إنما تستدعي امکان حصول الفعل في الزمان السابق لو كانت متقدمة على الفعل في ثاني الحال، أما إذا كانت على ايقاع الفعل في ثاني الحال، فلا يستدعي امكان الايقاع الذي في ثاني الحال، ولا يستدعي . الفعل في الحال، ولا يلزم من امكان الايقاع المذكور في الحال امكان الفعل في الحال حتى يلزم المحال، ألا ترى أن القدرة القديمة على ايقاع المقدورات مما لا تزال محققة في الأزل مع امتناع وجود المقدورات فيه وعلى هذا الجواب يرد بأن الايقاع في ثاني الحال أما نفس حصول الفعل في الوجود، فهو محال في الحال كالحصول فلا يكون متعلق القدرة بالتعلق المعنوي، وأما غيره فيحتاج إلى إيقاع آخر لأنه ممكن حال حاصل بتأثير القدرة في ظاهره مطابقة للسؤال بلا ريبة، وأما ما قيل في تقريره من أن القدرة متعلقة بالايقاع المتقدم على الوقوع زماناً فيكفي إمكان الايقاع
امكان
قوله : ( فإن قيل إلخ) حاصله أن القدرة في الحال متعلقة بالايقاع المتقدم على الوقوع زماناً فيكفي إمكان الايقاع في الحال ولا يستدعي هذا امكان الوقوع فيها، فتأمل.

وجدت في حال كانت متعلقة بوجود الفعل في ذلك الحال حتى يلزم امكان وجوده فيه، بل نقول : ( القدرة في الحال ) إنما هي ( على ايقاع الفعل في ثاني الحال وهو) أي تعلق القدرة السابقة بالفعل على هذا الوجه ( لا يستدعي امكانه ) أي امكان الفعل ( في الحال بل في ثاني الحال ) فلا يضرنا ما ذكرتم من أن العفل ليس بممكن قبل حدوثه في جواز كون القدرة موجودة قبله ( قلنا : الايقاع ) الذي هو تأثير القدرة الحادثة في الفعل وايجادها إياه على رأيكم ) إن كان نفس الفعل) على معنى أن التأثير في الفعل هو عين حصول الأثر الذي هو الفعل ( فمحال ) أي فالايقاع محال (في الحال لما ذكرنا من أن حصول الفعل مستحيل قبل زمان حدوثه ( وإن كان غيره عاد الكلام (فيه لأن الايقاع ممكن حادث فلا بد له من تأثير القدرة فيه فللايقاع ايقاع آخر ( ولزم التسلسل ) بأن يكون بين القدرة والفعل ايقاعات وتأثيرات غير متناهية لا يقال : الايقاع أمر اعتباري فلا حاجة به إلى ايقاع آخر لأنا نقول اتصاف الموقع بصفة ...
His saying: (Then if it is said etc.) is a prevention of his saying 'then power over it is not existing'. It is that it is permissible for power to exist in the time prior to the existence of the object of power, while the act is not possible at that time, on the basis that its connection (ta‘alluq) is in the second moment, and the possibility of the object of power only entails the connection because it is at the time of the act's existence, not at the time of power's existence. So what was mentioned of impossibility from power being prior and not prior does not follow. This is in accordance with what the commentator (al-Shāriḥ) determined. But it is replied to that the mention of iqā‘ (bringing about) then is superfluous; it suffices to say that power over the occurrence of the act in the second moment does not require its possibility in the present moment, etc. And power has two connections: a meaningful connection (ta‘alluq ma‘nawī) – it is said 'So-and-so is powerful over it', i.e. able to do and leave it, and by it the object of power is distinguished in relation to the powerful one. This cannot be delayed from power, and that is what we meant by the earlier statement that the non-existence of the object of power entails the non-existence of power. And there will come in the commentator's words that the existence of power without this connection is repugnant to intuition. And a connection by which existence is produced, which is delayed from the connection of will, and its delay after the existence of power is permissible. The discussion concerns the first, not the second. And my formulation of this objection is that the prior power requires the possibility of the act's occurrence in the prior time only if it were prior to the act in the second moment. But if it is for the bringing about (iqā‘) of the act in the second moment, then it does not require the possibility of bringing about in that second moment, nor does it require the act in the present moment. And the possibility of that mentioned bringing about in the present moment does not entail the possibility of the act in the present moment such that impossibility follows. Do you not see that the eternal power over the bringing about of objects of power is still realized in preeternity despite the impossibility of the existence of objects of power in it? Upon this answer it is replied that the bringing about in the second moment: if it is exactly the act's occurrence in existence, then it is impossible in the present moment, like the occurrence, so it would not be a connection of power with the meaningful connection. And if it is other than that, then the discussion returns to it, because the bringing about is a possible originated thing, so it must have an effect of power in it, so for the bringing about there is another bringing about, and an infinite regress follows, such that there would be between power and the act unlimited bringings about and effects. It cannot be said: the bringing about is a mental consideration, so it does not need another bringing about, because we say: the description of the one who brings about with the attribute... [text cut off]
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the previous proof and its refutation. The objector argues that it is possible for power to exist before the act because the connection (ta‘alluq) of power is not to the act at that moment but to the bringing about (iqā‘) of the act in a later moment. Thus the act does not need to be possible at the earlier time. The commentator then explains that power has two types of connection: a meaningful connection (ta‘alluq ma‘nawī) which is the ability to do or leave, and which cannot be delayed; and a productive connection (ta‘alluq yatarattab ‘alayhi al-wujūd) which can be delayed. The objection only concerns the second. Then the refutation is given: If the bringing about (iqā‘) is exactly the act's existence, then it is impossible at the earlier time. If it is something else, then that bringing about itself is an originated possible that requires another effect of power, leading to an infinite regress. The objection that iqā‘ is merely mental consideration is rejected because even mental considerations can lead to regress, but the text is cut off.

Key Terms
الايقاع
التعلق المعنوي
التعلق الإيجادي
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that by distinguishing the act from its 'bringing about' (iqa'), the problem of power preceding the act is solved cleanly.
Correction: The refutation shows that iqa' either reduces to the act itself (and thus faces the same impossibility) or it is a distinct entity that itself requires an effect of power, leading to an infinite regress.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, if the bringing about (iqā‘) is different from the act, what problem arises?
A) The bringing about is impossible in the prior moment.
B) It leads to an infinite regress of bringings about.
C) It makes power unnecessary.
D) The act becomes impossible in the later moment.

Key Takeaway
The objection that power can be prior via iqa' fails because iqa' either is the act (impossible earlier) or leads to an infinite regress of bringings about.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you delay a good deed, do you assume your current intention (niyya) and ability are enough for the future act? How does this passage's argument about iqā‘ expose the flaw in assuming power over a future act without present possibility?
?
The passage distinguishes between meaningful ability and productive action. In your life, where do you confuse being able 'in principle' with actually bringing about change? What would it mean to bridge that gap today, not just intend?
?
Al-Jurjani shows that making iqā‘ a mental consideration does not escape the regress. Where do you hide behind 'mental intentions' to avoid the real effects of your actions? What would it cost you to make your iqā‘ real—to actually bring about what you claim to intend?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
8 min
Two Interpretations of 'Act Before Act is Impossible'
Distinguishes two interpretations of impossibility and explains their logical status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possible interpretations of 'the act before the act is impossible' and why the impossibility is conditional, not absolute.

Core Idea
Impossibility is often conditional on a composite, not the act itself.

Before we can assess whether power can precede the act, we must first clarify what 'the act before the act is impossible' truly means. This passage distinguishes two interpretations: one that is contradictory due to temporal conditions, and another that is perfectly possible. Understanding this distinction reveals that the impossibility does not directly negate power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two distinct interpretations introduced by 'fa-inna-hu qad yurādu bihī' and 'wa-qad yurādu bihī'.
2
Identify the two meanings
Find the two interpretations: the first involves a time stipulated to be before the act, the second involves a time empty of non-existence. Write them down in your own words.
The first is a conditional impossibility (composite), the second is not impossible at all.
3
Analyze the first interpretation
Notice how the author shows that the impossibility arises from the combination of the act with the assumption that the time is prior and concurrent with non-existence, not from the act alone.
The act itself is possible (imkān dhātī), and the impossibility is accidental (imtināʿ ghayrī).
4
Analyze the second interpretation
Understand that the second interpretation simply posits the act in a time without any condition of non-existence, making it possible and free of contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه نظر يرجع ذلك النظر إلى تحقيق معنى قوله حصول الفعل قبل الفعل محال فإنه قد يراد به أن حصول الفعل في زمان بشرط كونه قبل الفعل محال فلا كلام فيه إذ لا شك أنه تناقض لاستلزامه أن يكون ذلك الزمان متقدماً على الفعل وأن لا يكون متقدماً عليه بل معه واستلزامه أيضاً اجتماع وجود الفعل وعدمه معاً لكن هذا المحال لم يلزم من وجود الفعل في ذلك الزمان وحده حتى يلزم امتناعه فيه بل منه مع فرض كون ذلك الزمان قبل الفعل مقارناً لعدمه فيكون هذا المجموع محالاً دون الفعل وحده بل هو ممكن في ذاته قطعاً فلا يتصف بالامتناع الذاتي أصلاً بل بالامتناع الغيري وذلك لا ينافي تعلق القدرة به وقد يراد به معنى آخر وهو وجود الفعل في زمان عدم الفعل لا بأن يجتمع فيه مع عدمه بل بأن يفرض خلوه أي خلو ذلك الزمان عن عدم الفعل ويفرض وقوع الفعل فيه بدله وأنه غير محال في نفسه ولا يستلزم محالاً أيضاً قوله: (لأنا نقول إلخ) يعني أن الإيقاع وإن لم يحتج أيضاً باعتبار الوجود المحمولي إلى ايقاع آخر لكنه محتاج باعتبار الوجود الرابطي ولا يمكن أن يقال: يجوز أن ينتهي إلى إيقاع قديم لأنه يستلزم قدرة الفعل لأن كل إيقاع مع إيقاع آخر وهو أثره لأن الإيقاع مع الوقوع قوله: (بشرط (كونه أي كون زمان حصول الفعل وهو أليق ببيان الشارح حيث جعل اللازم أولاً: كون ذلك الزمان متقدماً على الفعل وأن لا يكون متقدماً أو كون الفعل وحينئذ يكون اللازم أولاً اجتماع وجود الفعل وعدمه وثانياً: كون زمان الفعل متقدماً وغير متقدم قوله: (وأنه غير محال) فاللازم على هذا فيما نحن فيه أن تكون القدرة المتقدمة حال تقدمها ممكناً مقارنتها للفعل وذلك ليس بمحال فإن الجسم الأسود حال سواده يمكن اتصافه بالبياض وإنما المحال امکان اتصافه بالبياض بشرط اتصافه بالسواد لأنه المستلزم لامكان النقيضين المحض بأن اللازم هاهنا وقوع أمور اعتبارية غير متناهية في زمان تعلق القدرة بالفعل ووجوده وهو زمان متناه ووقوع الأمور الغير المتناهية في زمان متناه محال أيضاً نعم يمكن أن يقال: إنما يلزم التسلسل لو لم ينته إلى مرجح قديم فليتأمل قوله: (فإنه قد يراد به إلخ) ولك أن تقرر هكذا إن أردت بقولك حصول الفعل قبل الفعل محال استحالة حصوله في الزمان المتقدم قبل حصوله في الزمان المتأخر فلا نسلم الاستحالة وإن أردت استحالة حصوله قبل حصوله مطلقاً أي من غير تقييده بالزمان المتأخر فمسلم لكن هذا المحال لم يلزم من مجرد حصول الفعل في الزمان المقتدم بل من حصوله فيه مقارناً لعدمه فيجوز تعلق القدرة به قبل حدوثه على هذا الوجه وذلك الذي ذكرناه من أن الفعل قبله محال بشرط كونه قبل الفعل وليس بمحال إذا لم يؤخذ بذلك الشرط كقعود زيد فإنه محال بشرط قيامه أي يمتنع كونه قائماً قاعداً معاً فيكون الاجتماع لا القعود في نفسه ولا يمتنع قعوده في زمان قيامه فإنه لا يستحيل أن يعدم القيام ويوجد بدله القعود
And in it there is a consideration; that consideration refers to investigating the meaning of his statement: 'The occurrence of the act before the act is impossible.' For it may be intended thereby that the occurrence of the act in a time, with the condition that it be before the act, is impossible. There is no dispute about this, for there is no doubt that it is a contradiction, because it necessitates that that time be prior to the act and that it not be prior to it but rather with it, and it also necessitates the simultaneous existence and non-existence of the act. But this impossible thing did not follow from the existence of the act in that time alone, such that its impossibility in it would follow, but rather from it together with the assumption that that time is before the act and concurrent with its non-existence. So this composite is impossible, not the act alone; rather it is possible in itself absolutely, so it is not characterized by essential impossibility at all, but by accidental impossibility, and that does not conflict with power attaching to it. And it may be intended by it another meaning, namely the existence of the act in a time of the non-existence of the act — not such that it coexists in it with its non-existence, but such that it is assumed to be empty, i.e., that time empty of the non-existence of the act, and that the act is assumed to occur in it instead, and that it is not impossible in itself nor does it entail an impossibility. His statement: 'For we say, etc.' means that the bringing about, even if it does not need, with respect to predicative existence, another bringing about, yet it needs with respect to relational existence, and it cannot be said: it is permissible that it terminate at an eternal bringing about because it would necessitate the power of the act, since every bringing about is with another bringing about and it is its effect, because bringing about is with occurrence. His statement: 'with the condition that it be' — i.e., that the time of the act's occurrence — and he is more appropriate for explaining the commentator where he made the necessary entailment first: that that time be prior to the act and that it not be prior, or that the act [be prior]. And then the necessary entailment is first: the simultaneous existence and non-existence of the act, and second: that the time of the act be prior and not prior. His statement: 'and that it is not impossible' — thus the necessary entailment in our case is that the prior power, at the time of its priority, can be concurrent with the act, and that is not impossible. For the black body, at the time of its blackness, can be described by whiteness; the impossible is only the possibility of its being described by whiteness with the condition of its being described by blackness, because that entails the possibility of the two opposites. The absolute [impossibility] is that the necessary entailment here is the occurrence of infinite mental things in a time of the attachment of power to the act and its existence, which is a finite time, and the occurrence of infinite things in a finite time is also impossible. Yes, it can be said: infinite regress would only follow if it did not terminate at an eternal determinant, so let it be considered. His statement: 'For it may be intended thereby, etc.' — you may articulate it thus: if by your statement 'the occurrence of the act before the act is impossible' you mean the impossibility of its occurrence in the prior time before its occurrence in the later time, then we do not acknowledge the impossibility; and if you mean the impossibility of its occurrence before its occurrence absolutely, i.e., without restricting it to the later time, then it is granted, but this impossible thing does not follow from the mere occurrence of the act in the prior time, but from its occurrence in it concurrent with its non-existence. Thus it is permissible for power to attach to it before its occurrence in this manner. And that which we mentioned, that the act before it is impossible with the condition that it be before the act, and it is not impossible if not taken with that condition, like Zayd's sitting: it is impossible with the condition of his standing, i.e., it is impossible for him to be standing and sitting together, so the impossibility is the combination, not sitting itself, and his sitting in the time of his standing is not prevented, for it is not impossible to remove standing and bring sitting in its place.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. The objector claims that the act before its occurrence is impossible, so power cannot attach to it before its time. The commentator begins by distinguishing two interpretations of 'the act before the act is impossible'. First, if it means the act existing in a time that is stipulated to be before the act, then it is indeed contradictory and impossible because it would require the time to be both prior and not prior and would entail simultaneous existence and non-existence. However, the commentator argues that this impossibility results from the combination of the act with the assumption that the time is prior and that non-existence still holds, not from the act itself considered alone. The act in itself is possible and only accidentally impossible due to the temporal condition. Therefore, power can attach to it. Second, if the phrase means the act existing in a time that is empty of non-existence, without the act being in that time together with its non-existence, then this is not impossible at all. The commentator then explains that the Mu'tazilite claim that power must be before the act because otherwise an infinite regress would occur is refuted by noting that the regress only arises if there is no eternal determinant. He gives the example of Zayd's sitting: sitting while standing is impossible due to combination, but sitting in the time of standing (i.e., after standing is removed) is not impossible. Similarly, the act's occurrence in a prior time is not impossible if the time is not assumed to be simultaneously before and with the act.

Key Terms
الامتناع الذاتي
الامتناع الغيري
الفعل
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any impossibility before the act is absolute and prevents power.
Correction: The impossibility is accidental due to a temporal condition; the act itself is possible and power can attach to it.

Checkpoint
According to the first interpretation, why is the act before the act impossible?
A) Because the act cannot exist twice.
B) Because it requires time to be both prior and not prior, and act to exist and not exist simultaneously.
C) Because power cannot attach to it.
D) Because it is essentially impossible.

Key Takeaway
The act before the act is impossible only when considered with a contradictory temporal condition; the act itself remains possible in essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that the impossibility of an act before itself is only accidental and not essential, does this change how you view the capacity of God's power to create what might seem logically impossible to you? What specific belief about God's omnipotence do you need to adjust in light of this distinction?
?
The passage separates the act itself from the condition attached to it. In your own life, how often do you mistake a conditional difficulty for an absolute impossibility? What is one thing you have given up on because you thought it was essentially impossible, when in reality it was only accidentally hindered by a condition you could change?
?
If you truly internalized that what is possible in essence is not barred by accidental impossibilities (unless they are real), how would that affect your dua (supplication) or your trust in God's ability to bring about change in your heart, relationships, or circumstances? Will you commit tonight to praying for something you previously thought was impossible for you?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
10 min
Power Before the Act: Refuting Infinite Regress
Refutes the objection that power cannot precede the act due to infinite regress, using the example of Zayd's sitting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the refutation of infinite regress and the distinction between predicative and relational existence defend the possibility of power before the act.

Core Idea
Power can precede the act because the impossibility is conditional and does not entail an infinite regress if terminated at an eternal determinant.

Having clarified the two interpretations, the text now addresses the Mu'tazilite objection that power cannot precede the act because it would lead to an infinite regress. It argues that the regress only follows if there is no eternal determinant, and that the bringing about (īqāʿ) requires relational existence, but this does not negate the possibility. The example of Zayd's sitting clarifies the point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the refutation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the terms 'predicative existence' (wujūd maḥmūlī) and 'relational existence' (wujūd rābiṭī).
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Understand the distinction between predicative and relational existence
The author says that the bringing about (īqāʿ) does not need another bringing about in predicative existence, but it does in relational existence. Grasp this distinction.
Predicative existence refers to the existence of the act itself; relational existence refers to the connection between power and act.
3
Analyze the infinite regress argument
The objector claims that if power precedes the act, there must be an infinite chain of bringing about. The author responds that the regress can terminate at an eternal determinant (murjīḥ qadīm). Also note the point about infinite mental things in finite time being impossible, but that is avoided by the eternal determinant.
Think about why an eternal determinant stops the regress.
4
Study the example of Zayd's sitting
The example shows that sitting while standing is impossible due to the combination, but sitting in the time of standing (i.e., after standing is removed) is not impossible. This analogizes how the act before its time is not impossible if the condition is removed.
Relate this back to the two interpretations from the previous lesson.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If power can precede the act, what does that mean for the way you prepare your heart before you act? Are you quick to demand immediate results, or can you trust that your preparation is valid even before the outcome appears?
?
The example of Zayd's sitting shows that an act can be possible in a time when its opposite exists, provided that opposite is removed. What is one major change you have been avoiding because you think it is 'impossible' right now? What condition are you assuming that actually makes it impossible, and can that condition be removed?
?
The infinite regress is stopped by an eternal determinant. Where have you been looking for a never-ending chain of reasons to act or change, instead of anchoring yourself in something eternal? Are you waiting for a perfect sequence of circumstances, or can you act now with a higher intention?

Mu'tazilite Views on Power Before the Act and Their First Proof
Classification and refutation of Mu'tazilite views on power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite division on when power exists relative to the act, their first proof that power means bringing into existence, and the Ash'ari response that challenges the efficacy of originated power.

Core Idea
Power before the act or with it? The Mu'tazila disagreed, and their proof raised a deeper issue about causality.

The Mu'tazila were not monolithic on the question of when power exists relative to the act. While some agreed with the Ash'ari that power is concurrent with the act, most held that power must precede the act. This led to further disagreements on whether power continues after the act occurs. In this passage, we see their classification and the first of their proofs, along with the Ash'ari response that challenges the very nature of causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with a classification of Mu'tazilite views.
2
Identify the two subgroups on continuance
The passage mentions two groups among those Mu'tazila who hold power precedes the act. One says power must remain until the act exists, the other allows power to cease. Find these and note their reasoning.
The first group compares power's continuance to a specific constitution (al-binya al-makhsusa).
3
Examine the first proof
The Mu'tazila's first proof is that power's attachment means 'bringing into existence' (ijad), and bringing into existence what already exists is impossible. Therefore, power must precede the act.
This is the argument from 'tahsil al-hasil' (acquiring what is already obtained).
4
Understand the Ash'ari response
The response has two layers: first, the premise that originated power is truly effective is denied. Second, even if granted, 'bringing into existence' can be reinterpreted as the act's existence being dependent on the bringer at the time of bringing, not a second existence. The priority is essential, not temporal.
This response defends the Ash'ari view that power is concurrent with the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد وافق الشيخ في أن القدرة الحادثة مع الفعل كثير من المعتزلة كالنجار ومحمد بن عيسى وابن الراوندي وأبي عيسى الوراق وغيرهم وقالت المعتزلة أي أكثرهم القدرة قبل الفعل وتتعلق به حينئذ ويستحيل تعلقها بالفعل حال حدوثه ثم اختلفوا في بقاء القدرة فمنهم من قال ببقائها حال وجود الفعل وإن لم تكن القدرة الباقية قدرة عليه أي على ذلك الفعل لامتناع تعلقها به حال وجوده لكن يجب بقاؤه إلى زمان وجود مقدورها فإنها شرط لوجود المقدور كالبنية المخصوصة المشروطة في وجود الأفعال المقدورة ومنهم من نفاه أي وجوب البقاء وجوز انتفاء القدرة حال وجود الفعل كما جوزوا كلهم انتفاء الفعل حال وجود القدرة ودليلهم على أن القدرة وتعلقها بالفعل إنما هو قبله لا معه وجوه الأول إن تعلق القدرة بالفعل معناه الإيجاد وإيجاد الموجود محال لأنه تحصيل الحاصل بل يجب أن يكون الإيجاد قبل الوجود ولهذا صح أن يقال أوجده فوجد قلنا هذا مبني على أن القدرة الحادثة مؤثرة وهو ممنوع وعلى تقدير تسليمه نقول إيجاده أي إيجاد الموجود بذلك الوجود الذي هو أثر ذلك الإيجاد جائز بمعنى أن يكون ذلك الوجود الذي هو به موجود في زمان الإيجاد مستنداً إلى الموجد ومتفرعاً على إيجاده والمستحيل هو إيجاد الموجود بوجود آخر وتحقيقه ما مر من أن التأثير مع حصول الأثر بحسب الزمان وإن كان متقدما عليه بحسب الذات وهذا التقدم هو المصحح لاستعمال الفاء بينهما
And many of the Mu'tazila agreed with the Master (al-Shaykh) that originated power is with the act, such as al-Najjar, Muhammad ibn Isa, Ibn al-Rawandi, and Abu Isa al-Warraq, and others. And the Mu'tazila — i.e., most of them — said: power is before the act and attaches to it at that time, and it is impossible for it to attach to the act at the moment of its occurrence. Then they differed about the continuance of power. Among them were those who said that it remains at the time of the act's existence, even if the remaining power is not power over it, i.e., over that act, because of the impossibility of its attaching to it at the time of its existence, but its continuance is necessary until the time of the existence of its object, for it is a condition of the existence of the object, like the specific constitution that is a condition for the existence of predicable acts. And among them were those who negated it, i.e., the necessity of continuance, and allowed the non-existence of power at the time of the act's existence, just as they all allowed the non-existence of the act at the time of power's existence. And their proof that power and its attachment to the act is only before it, not with it, has several aspects. The first: that the attachment of power to the act means bringing into existence, and bringing into existence what already exists is impossible, because it is acquiring what is already obtained; rather, bringing into existence must be before existence, and for this reason it is correct to say, 'He brought it into existence, so it existed.' We said: this is based on the assumption that originated power is effective, which is denied. And on the supposition of granting it, we say: bringing it into existence — i.e., bringing into existence what exists — with that existence which is the effect of that bringing into existence, is permissible in the sense that that existence by which it exists at the time of bringing into existence is dependent on the bringer into existence and derived from his bringing into existence. The impossible is only bringing into existence what exists with another existence. Its verification is what has passed: that the effect has effect concurrent with the occurrence of the effect in terms of time, even if it is prior to it in terms of essence, and this priority is what justifies the use of 'fa' between them.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is both a classification and a refutation. First, the commentator notes that some Mu'tazila (al-Najjar, etc.) actually agreed with the Ash'ari view that power is concurrent with the act. But most Mu'tazila hold that power precedes the act and cannot attach at the moment of the act's occurrence. They then divide into two subgroups: one that says power must remain until the act's existence (as a condition, even if not as power over it), and another that denies the necessity of continuance. The commentator then presents their first proof: power's attachment means 'bringing into existence' (ijad), and bringing into existence what already exists is impossible (tahsil al-hasil). Hence, bringing into existence must be before existence. The response is twofold: first, the premise that originated power is effective (mu'aththira) is denied by the Ash'ari. Second, even if granted, 'bringing into existence' can be interpreted as the act's existence being dependent on the bringer at the time of bringing, not that the act exists twice. The priority is in essence, not time, as explained earlier.

المعتزلة
الإيجاد
تحصيل الحاصل
بقاء القدرة
المقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'bringing into existence what exists' is always impossible in any sense.
Correction: The Ash'ari response clarifies that it is only impossible if it means bringing into existence with another existence. It is permissible if the existence is the effect of that bringing, i.e., it is concurrent in time but dependent on the bringer.

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'ari response to the Mu'tazilite claim that power means bringing into existence, which must be before existence?
A) They agree that power must precede the act.
B) They deny that originated power is effective, and even if effective, bringing into existence can be simultaneous in time but prior in essence.
C) They argue that power is with the act, so bringing into existence is concurrent.
D) They claim that the act is created by God, not by originated power.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari response shows that the Mu'tazilite proof fails because it relies on a mistaken assumption about the efficacy of originated power and a misinterpretation of 'bringing into existence'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila stumbled over the idea that power could be concurrent with the act because it seemed to them like 'bringing into existence what already exists.' Is there an area of your faith where you have rejected a truth simply because your reason could not fit it into a neat timeline? Will you humble yourself and accept God's decree even when it seems 'impossible' to your mind?
?
If your power is not truly effective—if every action you take is simultaneously created by Allah—then what right do you have to be proud of anything you have done? Can you honestly look at your greatest achievement and say, 'This is Allah's doing, not mine,' not just with your tongue but with your heart? What would it take for you to live in that reality tomorrow?

The Second Mu'tazilite Proof: Power and the Enduring Act
Presents and refutes the Mu'tazilite analogy of power on originated and enduring acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'aris refute the Mu'tazilite analogy between the originated act and the enduring thing by distinguishing the need for a cause.

Core Idea
Only the originated act needs a cause; the enduring does not.

The Mu'tazilites argued that if power can attach to an act at the moment it occurs, it could also attach to an enduring thing. The Ash'ari response offers a crucial distinction: the originated act exists after non-existence and requires a cause, while the enduring does not. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the flow of the proof and response.
Notice how the proof sets up an entailment (ملازمة).
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite proof
The proof claims that if power attaches to the originated act, it must attach to the enduring because both are realized existences.
3
Examine the Ash'ari response
The response either accepts the consequent or distinguishes based on the need for a cause. Focus on the distinction as it breaks the analogy.
The distinction is that the originated is mawjud ba'da al-'adam (existent after non-existence) and needs a cause.
4
Reflect on the lesson's core idea
The key insight is that only things that come into existence require a power to bring them about; things that already exist do not.
This refutes the parallel between originated and enduring.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني إن جاز تعلق القدرة بالفعل الحادث حال حدوثه يلزم القدرة على الباقي حال بقائه والتالي باطل بيان الملازمة أن المانع من تعلق القدرة بالباقي ليس إلا كونه متحقق الوجود والحادث حال حدوثه متحقق الوجود أيضاً أو نقول وجود الباقي هو نفس الوجود حال الحدوث فلو تعلقت القدرة به حال الحدوث لتعلقت به حال البقاء لأن المتعلق واحد ولا تأثير لتعاقب الأوقات في أحكام الأنفس قلنا نلتزمه أي نلتزم تعلق القدرة بالباقي لدوام وجوده بدوام تعلق القدرة به أو نفرق بين الحادث والباقي بما تبطل به الملازمة المذكورة أعني باحتياج الموجود عن عدم إلى المقتضي لوجوده دون غيره وهو الباقي ومعناه أن الحادث هو الموجود بعد العدم فلو لم تتعلق به القدرة لبقي على عدمه
The second aspect: if it were permissible for power to attach to the originated act at the moment of its occurrence, then power over the enduring (al-baqi) would also be necessary at the moment of its continuance; but the consequent is false. The explanation of the entailment is that the preventer of power's attachment to the enduring is nothing but its being a realized existence, and the originated act at the moment of its occurrence is also a realized existence. Or we say: the existence of the enduring is the very existence at the moment of origination; so if power attached to it at the moment of origination, it would attach to it at the moment of continuance, because the object of attachment is one and the succession of times has no effect on the rulings of the essences. We said: we accept it — i.e., we accept that power attaches to the enduring for the continuance of its existence by the continuance of power's attachment to it — or we distinguish between the originated and the enduring in a way that invalidates the mentioned entailment, namely by the need of the existent after non-existence for the cause of its existence, unlike the other, which is the enduring. Its meaning is that the originated is the existent after non-existence; so if power did not attach to it, it would remain in its non-existence.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents the second Mu'tazilite proof and the Ash'ari response. The proof argues by analogy: if power could attach to the act at the moment of its origination, then it could also attach to an enduring thing at the moment of its continuance, because both are realized existences. Since we cannot have power over an enduring thing (e.g., to bring it back into existence once it exists), the original premise must be false. The response offers two options: either accept the consequent (agree that power attaches to the enduring, i.e., for its continuance) or make a distinction. The distinction is that the originated act exists after non-existence and needs a cause (mawjud ba'da al-'adam), while the enduring already exists and does not need a cause for its continued existence. Therefore, the analogy fails because the originated act requires power to bring it from non-existence, whereas the enduring does not.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If power can be concurrent with an act, it could also affect something already existing.
Correction: The Ash'ari response clarifies that only the originated act (existent after non-existence) needs a cause; the enduring does not, so the analogy fails.

Checkpoint
What distinction does the Ash'ari make to refute the Mu'tazilite analogy between the originated and the enduring?
A) The originated act is impossible, but the enduring is possible.
B) The originated act is a cause, while the enduring is an effect.
C) The originated act exists after non-existence and needs a cause, while the enduring already exists and does not.
D) Power cannot attach to either, so the analogy is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari refutation hinges on the fact that only things that come into existence (al-hadith) need a power, whereas things that already endure do not.

?
Consider an area of your life where you feel 'enduring'—perhaps a skill, a relationship, or a habit. Does this passage challenge you to see that even that continuity depends entirely on God's ongoing act of sustaining? What would it mean for you to consciously attribute your persistence to Him each day?
?
When you face something new or difficult, do you assume you have the power within yourself to handle it, or do you see yourself as a 'hadith' needing God's power to bring it forth? Commit to one specific area where you will actively depend on God's power rather than your own perceived capacity.

The First Mu'tazilite Proof: Power Must Be Before the Act
Mu'tazilite proof that power precedes act and Ash'ari rejoinder.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that power must precede the act because bringing into existence what already exists is impossible, and the Ash'ari response that reinterprets priority as essential rather than temporal.

Core Idea
Power brings into existence, and what already exists cannot be brought into existence again.

The Mu'tazilites argued that since power means bringing into existence, and bringing into existence what already exists is absurd (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil), power must be before the act. The Ash'aris respond by denying that created power is an efficient cause and, even if accepted, they argue that the priority is ontological, not temporal. This lesson unpacks that debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its flow. Then read the full English translation. Notice the two main parts: the Mu'tazilite proof and the Ash'ari response starting with 'We said' (qulna).
The passage uses technical terms like 'ijad' and 'ta'thir'. They will be explained in the commentary.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite argument
The Mu'tazilites claim: power's relation to the act is bringing into existence; bringing into existence what already exists is impossible; therefore power must be before the act. Find these elements in the text.
Note the use of 'fa' in the example 'awjada fa wujida' – they argue it implies temporal precedence.
3
Analyze the Ash'ari response
The response has two parts. First, it denies the premise that created power is an efficient cause (mu'aththir). Second, even accepting that premise, it reinterprets the meaning of priority: bringing into existence can be with the same existence as the effect, but the influence is prior in essence, not in time.
The key distinction is between temporal priority and essential priority (al-taqaddum bi al-dhat).
4
Understand the difference between temporal and essential priority
Reflect on how the Ash'aris use the concept of essential priority to justify the 'fa' in 'He brought it into existence, so it came into existence'. This is a sophisticated move that preserves the causal language without requiring temporal separation.
This distinction is a recurring theme in Ash'ari kalam when dealing with causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليلهم على أن القدرة وتعلقها بالفعل إنما هو قبله لا معه ) وجوه الأول : إن تعلق القدرة بالفعل (معناه الإيجاد وإيجاد الموجود محال لأنه تحصيل الحاصل بل يجب أن يكون الإيجاد قبل الوجود، ولهذا صح أن يقال : أوجده فوجد (قلنا ) : هذا مبني على أن القدرة الحادثة مؤثرة، وهو ممنوع وعلى تقدير تسليمه نقول : ( إيجاده ) أي إيجاد الموجود ( بذلك الوجود ) الذي هو أثر ذلك الإيجاد ) جائز بمعنى أن يكون ذلك الوجود ) الذي هو به موجود في زمان الإيجاد (مستنداً إلى الموجد ) ومتفرعاً على إيجاده، والمستحيل هو إيجاد الموجود بوجود آخر، وتحقيقه ما مر من أن التأثير مع حصول الأثر بحسب الزمان، وإن كان متقدما عليه بحسب الذات وهذا التقدم هو المصحح لاستعمال الفاء بينهما .
And their proof that power and its relation to the act is only before it, not with it, has aspects. The first: that the relation of power to the act means bringing into existence (ijad), and bringing into existence what already exists is impossible because it is obtaining the obtained; rather, bringing into existence must be before existence. Hence it is correct to say: 'He brought it into existence, so it came into existence.' We said: This is based on the premise that created power is an efficient cause (mu'aththir), and this is denied. And on the supposition of its acceptance, we say: bringing him into existence (i.e., bringing the existent into existence) with that existence which is the effect (athar) of that bringing into existence is permissible, in the sense that that existence by which he exists at the time of bringing into existence is dependent on the bringer-into-existence (al-mujid) and derived from his bringing into existence. What is impossible is bringing into existence what exists with another existence. Its verification is what has passed, that the influence (ta'thir) is with the occurrence of the effect in terms of time, although it is prior to it in terms of essence, and this priority is what justifies the use of the particle 'fa' (then) between them.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents the first of the Mu'tazilite proofs for the claim that power relates to the act only before the act, not with it, and the Ash'ari response. The proof is: the relation of power to the act is bringing into existence (ijad); but bringing into existence what already exists is impossible (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil); therefore power must be before the act. The response (qulna) first denies the premise that created power is an efficient cause (mu'aththir) — a key Ash'ari position. Even if one grants that premise, the response refines the concept: bringing into existence an existent with the very existence that is the effect of that bringing into existence is permissible. The impossible is only bringing into existence with a different existence. The response then invokes an earlier discussion about influence (ta'thir) being simultaneous with the effect in time but prior in essence (bi-ḥasb al-dhat), and this essential priority justifies the use of the word 'fa' (then) in 'He brought it into existence, so it came into existence.' Thus the proof fails to establish temporal priority.

Key Terms
الإيجاد
الأثر
التأثير
الموجد
الفاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari response denies any causal relationship between power and act.
Correction: The Ash'ari response does not deny causality; it reinterprets the priority as essential (ontological) rather than temporal, and it denies that created power is an efficient cause in the sense of producing the act independently.

Checkpoint
What is the first part of the Ash'ari response to the Mu'tazilite proof?
A) They agree that power is before the act
B) They deny that created power is an efficient cause
C) They say the act is eternal
D) They claim power is after the act

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite proof fails because it conflates temporal priority with the kind of essential priority that justifies the 'fa' in the statement 'He brought it into existence, so it came into existence.'

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you rely more on your own power and planning than on Allah's creation of the results? This lesson shows that your power (qudra) is not an efficient cause in the absolute sense. How does that challenge the way you attribute success or failure in your life? Will you change your reliance today?
?
The Mu'tazilites insisted power must be before the act to protect God's justice. The Ash'aris responded that temporal priority is not necessary. Do you ever assume that your actions must be temporally prior to their effects, thereby forgetting that Allah creates both you and your action in each moment? What would it mean for your daily prayers and efforts if you truly believed that your power is simultaneous with the act, derived from Allah's creation?
?
This lesson's key takeaway is the distinction between temporal and essential priority. In your life, do you measure your relationship with Allah by temporal sequence (e.g., I pray then He responds) or by essential dependence (e.g., even now my existence is from Him)? Which understanding leads to deeper gratitude and humility? What step can you take today to live more in the second understanding?

Second Proof and Two Ash'ari Responses: Acceptance and Distinction
Presents the proof and two Ash'ari responses: acceptance and distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite second proof for power over the enduring and two Ash'ari responses: accepting the consequence with a qualification, and distinguishing between temporal and enduring based on the need for a ground.

Core Idea
The Ash'aris agree that power relates to the enduring, but only as maintenance, not origination.

The Mu'tazilites argue that if power can relate to a temporal act at its moment, it must also relate to the enduring act at its moment, because both are realized existents. The Ash'aris have multiple responses. In this lesson, we examine the first two: accepting the consequence with a nuance, and drawing a distinction based on the need for a causal ground.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and its connection
Read from the beginning through the explanation of the connection. Note how the Mu'tazilites argue that the only hindrance to power's relation to the enduring is its being a realized existent, which also applies to the temporal act at its moment.
The proof is an argument by analogy.
2
Study the first Ash'ari response (acceptance)
Read the 'قلنا : نلتزمه' section. Notice that they accept that power relates to the enduring, but they qualify it: power relates for the duration of existence by a continuous relation, not a new relation at each moment.
This is a qualified acceptance, not a full endorsement of the Mu'tazilite conclusion.
3
Examine the second response (distinction)
Read the 'أو نفرق' section. Understand the key difference: the temporal comes from non-existence and needs a ground (muqtadi) for its existence, while the enduring does not because it already exists.
The distinction invalidates the assumption that the cases are analogous.
4
Compare the two responses
Ask yourself how each response avoids the conclusion that power must be related to the enduring in the same way as to the temporal. Which response is more radical?
The first accepts the consequence but reinterprets it; the second rejects the analogy entirely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه ( الثاني : ( إن جاز تعلق القدرة بالفعل الحادث حال حدوثه ( يلزم القدرة على الباقي ( حال ،بقائه والتالي باطل بيان الملازمة أن المانع من تعلق القدرة بالباقي ليس إلا كونه متحقق الوجود والحادث حال حدوثه متحقق الوجود أيضاً، أو نقول : وجود الباقي هو نفس الوجود حال الحدوث، فلو تعلقت القدرة به حال الحدوث لتعلقت به حال البقاء، لأن المتعلق واحد ولا تأثير لتعاقب الأوقات في أحكام الأنفس (قلنا : نلتزمه ) أي نلتزم تعلق القدرة بالباقي (لدوام وجوده بدوام تعلق القدرة ) به ( أو نفرق بين الحادث والباقي بما تبطل به الملازمة المذكورة أعني باحتياج الموجود عن عدم إلى المقتضي ( لوجوده ( دون غيره) وهو الباقي ومعناه أن الحادث هو الموجود بعد العدم، فلو لم تتعلق به القدرة لبقي على عدمه، وقد فرضنا وجوده هذا خلف بخلاف الباقي فإنه كان موجوداً حال الحدوث فلو لم تتعلق به القدرة لبقي على الوجود، وليس بمحال لكونه مطابقاً للواقع (أو ننقض) دليلهم ( أولاً بتأثير العلم في الاتقان ) فإن المؤثر في إتقان الفعل وأحكامه هو العلم أو العالمية عندهم، ولم يشترطوا مقارنة شيء منهما للاتقان حالة البقاء، وإن كان ذلك مشروطاً عندهم حال حدوثه (و) ثانيا : بتأثير الفعل (في كون الفاعل فاعلاً) فإن الفعل مؤثر في اتصاف الفاعل بكونه فاعلاً حال الحدوث، وبتقدير كون الفعل باقياً عندهم لايؤثر في اتصافه بالفاعلية حال البقاء (و) ننقضه ثالثا بمقارنة ( الإرادة إذ يوجبونها ) أي يوجبون مقارنتها للموجود ) حال الحدوث دون البقاء) فلم يلزم من عدم المقارنة حال البقاء عدم المقارنة حال ،الحدوث، فكذا الحال في القدرة. قال الآمدي : ولو راموا الفرق بين هذه الصور الثلاث وبين القدرة لم يجدوا إليه سبيلاً.
The second aspect: If it were permissible for power to be related to the temporal act at the moment of its occurrence, then power over the enduring (al-baqi) would follow at the moment of its endurance. The consequent is false. The explanation of the connection is that the only hindrance to power's relation to the enduring is its being a realized existent, and the temporal act at the moment of its occurrence is also a realized existent. Or we say: the existence of the enduring is the very same existence as at the moment of origination; so if power were related to it at the moment of origination, it would be related to it at the moment of endurance, because the object of relation is one and the succession of times has no effect on the judgments of essences. (We said: We accept it) i.e., we accept power's relation to the enduring for the duration of its existence through the duration of power's relation to it. (Or we differentiate between the temporal and the enduring by something that invalidates the mentioned connection, namely, by the need of that which comes into existence from non-existence for a ground (muqtadi) for its existence, unlike the other, which is the enduring. Its meaning is that the temporal is the existent after non-existence; so if power were not related to it, it would remain in non-existence, but we have assumed its existence — this is a contradiction. In contrast, the enduring was existent at the moment of origination; so if power were not related to it, it would remain in existence, and this is not impossible because it corresponds to reality. Or we refute their proof: first, by the effect of knowledge in precision (itqan), for the effective factor in the precision and perfection of the act is knowledge or the knower, according to them, and they did not require the accompaniment of either of them for precision during the state of endurance, although that is required by them at the moment of origination. Second, by the effect of the act in making the agent an agent, for the act is effective in qualifying the agent as an agent at the moment of origination, but on the supposition that the act endures, according to them, it does not affect his qualification as an agent during endurance. Third, we refute it by the accompaniment of will (irada), for they require its accompaniment of the existent at the moment of origination but not during endurance. So the lack of accompaniment during endurance does not entail the lack of accompaniment during origination. The same holds for power. Al-Amidi said: If they attempted to distinguish between these three cases and power, they would find no way to do so.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents the second Mu'tazilite proof and multiple Ash'ari rebuttals. The proof argues: if power could relate to a temporal act at its moment of occurrence, then it could also relate to an enduring (baqi) state at its moment of endurance, because both are realized existents; the consequent is false. The Ash'ari response offers three strategies: (1) Accept the consequence — power does relate to the enduring, but only in the sense of maintaining it, not originating it. (2) Distinguish between temporal (ḥadith) and enduring (baqi): the temporal needs a ground (muqtadi) because it comes from non-existence, while the enduring does not; thus the analogy fails. (3) Refute by counterexamples: (a) knowledge (or the knower) is required for precision at origination but not at endurance, (b) the act is required for the agent's qualification as agent at origination but not at endurance, (c) will is required for the willed at origination but not at endurance. Since the Mu'tazilites accept these distinctions, they cannot consistently reject a similar distinction for power. Al-Amidī confirms that no distinction can be drawn. The passage then continues with further commentary in the sharh, but the text we have covers the core argument and responses.

Key Terms
القدرة
الباقي
الحادث
المقتضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'aris completely deny any relation of power to the enduring.
Correction: The Ash'aris accept that power relates to the enduring, but only in the sense of maintaining its existence, not originating it.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the distinction between the temporal and the enduring in the second Ash'ari response?
A) The temporal has no existence.
B) The temporal needs a ground because it comes from non-existence.
C) The enduring is eternal.
D) Power cannot relate to the enduring.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'aris have two preliminary responses to the second proof: qualified acceptance and a distinction based on the need for a ground.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly see your own existence as something that requires God's continuous maintenance, or do you subconsciously feel self-sufficient once you exist? What would change in your daily gratitude if you internalized the Ash'ari position that sustaining is distinct from creating?
?
The Mu'tazilite proof assumes similarity between origination and endurance. Have you ever equated your ability to maintain your habits or faith with your initial conversion or commitment? How does the Ash'ari distinction challenge your understanding of your need for God's power at every moment?
?
When you think about your faith (iman), do you assume that once you have it, it endures on its own? Or do you recognize that it needs continuous maintenance from God? How does this lesson force you to pray differently for your own steadfastness?

Refuting the Second Proof: Three Counterexamples from Knowledge, Act, and Will
Presents three counterexamples and al-Amidi's conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari refutation of the Mu'tazilite second proof using three counterexamples from knowledge, act, and will, and al-Amidi's conclusion.

Core Idea
If the Mu'tazilites accept that knowledge, act, and will have different requirements at origination and endurance, they cannot reject a similar distinction for power.

The Ash'aris further undermine the second proof by showing that the same logic would lead to unacceptable consequences in other domains. They provide three counterexamples: knowledge's effect on precision, the act's effect on agency, and will's accompaniment. Al-Amidi concludes that no distinction can save the Mu'tazilite position.

Guided Path
1
Read the three counterexamples
Read the 'أو ننقض' section, which lists three refutations: knowledge (itqan), act (being an agent), and will (irada). Notice that each is introduced with 'أولاً', 'ثانياً', 'ثالثاً'.
These are analogical refutations (nuqūḍ) meant to show inconsistency.
2
Understand the first example: knowledge
The Mu'tazilites hold that knowledge (or the knower) is required for the precision of an act at origination, but not at endurance. The Ash'aris argue that similarly, power's accompaniment should not be required at endurance.
The key is that the requirement differs between the two moments.
3
Understand the second example: act making the agent
The act qualifies the agent as an agent at origination, but on the supposition that the act endures, it does not continue to affect agency during endurance. So again, the requirement is not continuous.
4
Understand the third example: will
Will must accompany the willed at origination but not during endurance. Therefore, the lack of accompaniment during endurance does not imply lack during origination. The same applies to power.
This directly parallels the power case.
5
Read al-Amidi's conclusion
Read the final sentence by al-Amidi. He states that the Mu'tazilites cannot find a way to distinguish between these three cases and power.
This reinforces the strength of the refutation.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you accept logical distinctions in your own reasoning that you would not accept from others? When have you dismissed a valid argument from someone else because it felt inconvenient, even though you apply the same logic yourself? Commit today to holding your own reasoning to the same standard you demand of others.
?

What is one area where you demand perfection from others but excuse yourself? Write down one concrete step to align your actions with your professed beliefs before the day ends.
?

What belief or habit are you clinging to that you know deep down does not align with the Quran or Sunnah? Resolve to investigate it today with sincere intention to follow the truth.

Three Counterexamples Against the Necessity of Power Being Simultaneous with the Act
Al-Jurjani presents three counterexamples and the subsequent debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how three counterexamples from the Mu'tazilites' own principles undermine their claim that power must be simultaneous with the act, and how the debate unfolds with responses and rebuttals.

Core Idea
The opponent's own principles are used against them to show that power can precede the act.

This passage from al-Jurjani's commentary engages in a dialectical refutation (naqd) of a key Mu'tazilite argument for the simultaneity of power and act. By examining three cases where their own doctrines do not require simultaneity in the state of permanence, al-Jurjani forces them into inconsistency. The passage also records al-Amidi's confident assertion that no distinction can be made, and the subsequent responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note the dialectical flow: the counterexamples are first stated, then a response is given, then further rebuttals.
Pay attention to the structure: 'أو ننقض' introduces the three counterexamples, then 'قال الآمدي', then 'أجيب عن النقضين الأولين' etc.
2
Identify the three counterexamples
Find the three cases: (1) knowledge's effect on perfection, (2) the act's effect on being an agent, (3) the concomitance of will. Notice that in each case the opponent requires simultaneity at origination but not at permanence.
3
Understand the opponent's answer
The passage gives the opponent's response: for the first two, only the essence is required at origination, not the effect; for the third, they claim they do require permanence of will's concomitance. Follow the logic.
Al-Jurjani then provides further rebuttals to each point.
4
Examine the final defense and its rebuttal
The opponent offers a defense using the concept of 'state' (hal) to distinguish origination. Note how al-Jurjani dismisses this because they do not accept intermediate states.
This shows the depth of the dialectic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو ننقض دليلهم أولاً بتأثير العلم في الاتقان فإن المؤثر في إتقان الفعل وأحكامه هو العلم أو العالمية عندهم، ولم يشترطوا مقارنة شيء منهما للاتقان حالة البقاء، وإن كان ذلك مشروطاً عندهم حال حدوثه وثانيا بتأثير الفعل في كون الفاعل فاعلاً فإن الفعل مؤثر في اتصاف الفاعل بكونه فاعلاً حال الحدوث، وبتقدير كون الفعل باقياً عندهم لايؤثر في اتصافه بالفاعلية حال البقاء وننقضه ثالثا بمقارنة الإرادة إذ يوجبونها أي يوجبون مقارنتها للموجود حال الحدوث دون البقاء فلم يلزم من عدم المقارنة حال البقاء عدم المقارنة حال الحدوث، فكذا الحال في القدرة. قال الآمدي: ولو راموا الفرق بين هذه الصور الثلاث وبين القدرة لم يجدوا إليه سبيلاً. قوله: (أو ننقض دليلهم أولا إلخ) أجيب عن النقضين الأولين بأن ما يشترطون مقارنته بحال الحدوث هو ذات العلم والفعل، فلا يلزم منه القول بمقارنة تأثيرهما له، بل التأثير عندهم قبل حال الحدوث، فلا يلزمهم القول بمقارنته حال البقاء، كما لزم القائلين بأن تعلق القدرة بالفعل الحادث حال حدوثه، وعن الثالث بأنهم يلتزمون مقارنة الإرادة للمراد حال البقاء أيضاً، ويمكن أن يدفع الأول بأن تمام النقض لا يتوقف على قولهم بأن العلم أو العالمية مؤثر حال الحدوث دون البقاء، بل يكفي فيه أنهم يوجبون مقارنة أحدهما لحدوث الفعل دون بقائه فقول الناقض بتأثير العلم معناه بالعلم المؤثر ويرد الثاني أن المراد بحال الحدوث هاهنا حال حدوث الفعل المؤثر والفعل قبل حدوثه معدوم فلا يعقل تأثيره في شيء، فليس تأثيره عندهم إلا في حال حدوثه والثالث: أن وجوب المقارنة للمراد حال البقاء لا يقولون به وجوازها لا يقدح في النقض إذ يكفي فيه أنهم يجوزون عدم المقارنة حال البقاء، ولا يجوزون حال الحدوث، وقد يجاب عن النقض بأنه يجوز أن يكون مرادهم في الصور الثلث بحال الحدوث الحال الذي يكون الشيء فيه لا موجوداً ولا معدوماً بناء على القول بالحال فيحصل الفرق بينه وبين حال البقاء وفيه أنهم لا يثبتون للموجودات حالة الواسطة.
Or we refute their proof first by the effect of knowledge in perfection, for the effective factor in the perfection of the act and its soundness is knowledge or omniscience according to them, and they did not require the concomitance of either of them for perfection in the state of permanence, although that was required by them in the state of origination. And second, by the effect of the act in making the agent an agent, for the act is effective in qualifying the agent as being an agent in the state of origination, and supposing the act is permanent according to them, it does not affect his qualification as an agent in the state of permanence. And we refute it third by the concomitance of will, for they require it, i.e., they require its concomitance with the existent in the state of origination but not in permanence. So lack of concomitance in permanence does not entail lack of concomitance in origination, and likewise the case for power. Al-Amidi said: If they sought to distinguish between these three forms and power, they would not find a way to it. He said: (Or we refute their proof first, etc.) It was answered concerning the first two refutations that what they require to be concomitant in the state of origination is the essence of knowledge and act, so the statement of the concomitance of their effect with it does not follow; rather, their effect according to them is before the state of origination, so they are not required to affirm its concomitance in the state of permanence, as was required of those who said that the attachment of power to the originated act is in the state of its origination. And concerning the third, that they affirm the concomitance of will with the willed object in the state of permanence as well. And it is possible to rebut the first by saying that the completeness of the refutation does not depend on their statement that knowledge or omniscience is effective in the state of origination but not in permanence; rather, it is sufficient in it that they require the concomitance of one of them for the origination of the act but not for its permanence, so the statement of the refuter 'by the effect of knowledge' means by effective knowledge. And the second is rebutted by saying that what is meant by the state of origination here is the state of origination of the effective act, and the act before its origination is non-existent, so its effect on anything is not conceivable; thus its effect according to them is only in the state of its origination. And the third: that the obligation of concomitance with the willed object in the state of permanence they do not affirm, and its permissibility does not harm the refutation, for it is sufficient in it that they allow lack of concomitance in the state of permanence while not allowing it in the state of origination. And it may be answered concerning the refutation that it is possible that their meaning in the three forms by 'the state of origination' is the state in which the thing is neither existent nor non-existent, based on the doctrine of the state, so a distinction obtains between it and the state of permanence. But it is objected that they do not affirm for existents the intermediate state.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage presents three counterexamples (naqd) against the Mu'tazilite claim that power (qudra) must be simultaneous with the act, followed by answers defending the counterexamples. The author first states three cases where the opponent's own principles do not require simultaneity in the state of permanence (baqa') even though they require it in the state of origination (huduth): (1) knowledge (or omniscience) is effective in perfecting the act, but they do not require its concomitance in permanence; (2) the act itself makes the agent an agent only at origination, not at permanence; (3) will (irada) must attend the existent at origination but not at permanence. Al-Amidi then remarks that the opponent cannot distinguish these from the case of power. The commentator (al-Jurjani) then provides the opponent's answer: the first two counterexamples fail because what they require at origination is the essence of knowledge and act, not their effect; the effect occurs before origination. For will, they claim they do require its concomitance at permanence as well. Further rebuttals are given: the first counterexample stands because even if knowledge is not effective, its concomitance at origination suffices; the second is refuted because the act only becomes effective at origination; the third is refuted because the opponent does not require will's concomitance at permanence, only permits it. Finally, a possible defense by the opponent uses the notion of 'state' (hal) to distinguish origination as an intermediate state, but this is rejected because they do not accept intermediate states.

المقارنة
حال الحدوث
حال البقاء
النقض
الآمدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These counterexamples are meant to prove that power cannot be with the act.
Correction: They are not proving that; they are using the opponent's own principles to show inconsistency, forcing them to accept that power can precede the act.

Checkpoint
What is the main purpose of the three counterexamples (naqd) in this passage?
A) To prove that power must be simultaneous with the act
B) To refute the Mu'tazilite argument that power must be simultaneous with the act by using their own principles
C) To establish that knowledge and will are not effective
D) To show that power is not a real attribute

Key Takeaway
The three counterexamples demonstrate that the Mu'tazilite principle of simultaneity is inconsistent with their own doctrines regarding knowledge, act, and will, thereby undermining their argument for power's simultaneity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The opponent tried to escape the counterexample by making a fine distinction (essence vs. effect). Have you ever made a convenient distinction to avoid admitting a flaw in your own reasoning? What would it look like to accept the full logical weight of your principles?
?
The defense using the 'state' (hal) was rejected because the opponent did not accept that concept. Are there theological concepts you dismiss out of convenience because they would force you to rethink your position? What would it mean to seriously engage with ideas that challenge your current understanding?

Acts Needing and Not Needing an Instrument
States the division of possible acts according to Mu'tazila and Ash'ari position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili and Ash'ari views on the division of possible acts into those that require an instrument and those that do not, and the Ash'ari argument that temporal power does not extend to acts outside its locus.

Core Idea
Possible acts are divided into instrumental and non-instrumental, but Ash'aris limit power to its own locus.

This passage discusses the second branch of the classification of possible acts. The Mu'tazila agree that some acts require an instrument (like moving a stone) while others do not (like moving one's hand). The Ash'aris, however, argue that temporal power cannot relate to acts outside its locus, which has implications for understanding causality and divine action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English translation carefully, noting the structure of the classification and the Ash'ari position.
Pay attention to the unclear phrases marked with [?].
2
Identify the two types of acts

3
Understand the Ash'ari position
Focus on the sentence about Ash'aris: temporal power does not relate to what is outside its locus. Consider why this is important.
4
Examine the commentary on direct and generated acts
The passage explains that both movements are direct, but the stone's movement requires intention and is not generated from the hand movement. Understand the definition of a generated act.
5
Reflect on the division's basis
Consider why the division is a branch of the precedence of power.

The second branch: They agreed that the possible acts are divided into what does not need, in its occurrence, an instrument, like those subsisting in the locus, i.e., acts subsisting in the locus of power like the movement of the hand, and into what needs in its occurrence an instrument, like those external to it, i.e., acts external to the locus of power like the movement of a stone by moving the hand. The agent and from his saying: 'does not relate to what is outside its locus' because the relation is with the act, and the external act if it occurs after the death of this [agent] reveals that the division is a branch of the precedence of power.

Commentary
This passage presents the second branch concerning the classification of possible acts. The Ash'aris, however, hold that temporal power does not relate to acts outside its locus. The generated act is what another act necessitates. The division shows that the external act relates to the precedence of power.

Abu Hudhayl on Power Over Heart and Limbs
Abu Hudhayl distinguishes timing of power for heart and limb acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hudhayl's distinction between the timing of power over acts of the heart and acts of the limbs, and why al-Amidi considered such debates baseless.

Core Idea
Power over heart acts is simultaneous; power over limb acts must precede.

Abu Hudhayl al-‘Allaf, an early Mu'tazilite theologian, proposed a precise distinction regarding when divine power (qudra) is present in relation to human acts. He claimed that for internal acts of the heart, such as belief or intention, power exists simultaneously with the act, not before. For external acts of the limbs, however, power must precede the act. This passage presents his view and al-Amidi's sharp criticism, who dismisses the entire debate as baseless and a waste of time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read Abu Hudhayl's statement in Arabic and English, noticing his two-part claim about heart and limb acts.
Pay attention to the specific words for 'with' (ma‘a) and 'before' (qabla).
2
Understand the distinction
Identify what Abu Hudhayl says about the timing of power for heart acts (simultaneous) versus limb acts (preceding). Why might this distinction matter in theology?
3
Consider al-Amidi's critique
Read al-Amidi's dismissal and reflect on why he considers such debates baseless. What does this tell us about the nature of Islamic theological discourse?
Al-Amidi's response is a strong rejection of the entire line of inquiry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الرابع : قال أبو الهذيل : ( العلاف القدرة على أفعال القلوب (معها ولا يجوز تقدمها عليها (و) القدرة ) على أفعال الجوارح ) يجب تكون (قبلها ) قال الآمدي : هذه وأمثالها من الاختلافات التي لا مستند لها يظهر فسادها بأوائل النظر فيها والاشتغال بها تضييع للزمان في غيرهم فلذلك أعرضنا عنها .
The fourth branch: Abu Hudhayl al-‘Allaf said: 'Power over acts of the heart is with them, and it is not permissible for it to precede them. And power over acts of the limbs must be before them.' Al-Amidi said: 'These and similar disagreements are those without basis; their corruption appears at first glance, and occupying oneself with them is a waste of time in other than them, so we have turned away from them.'
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a brief statement by Abu Hudhayl al-‘Allaf regarding the timing of power (qudra) in relation to two types of acts: acts of the heart (af‘al al-qulub), such as belief or intention, and acts of the limbs (af‘al al-jawarih), such as moving one's hand. He asserts that power over heart acts is simultaneous (ma‘a) with the act itself, meaning it cannot exist before the act. In contrast, power over limb acts must precede (qabla) the act. This distinction is presented without proof, and al-Amidi dismisses such debates as baseless, claiming their falsity is obvious to any beginner and that engaging with them is a waste of time. The lesson thus records a specific view and a critical evaluation.

Prevention and Capacity: The Core Disagreement
Presents the two positions and the first Mu'tazilite argument from necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari and Mu'tazilite positions on whether a prevented person is capable, and the Mu'tazilite distinction between inability and prevention.

Core Idea
Prevention opposes the act, not the ability.

This lesson introduces a fundamental debate about the nature of capacity when an act is prevented. The Ash'aris hold that power is simultaneous with the act, so no act means no power. The Mu'tazilites disagree, claiming that prevention only blocks the act, not the underlying ability, and support this with a rational distinction between inability ('ajz) and prevention (man').

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the quick shift from Ash'ari to Mu'tazilite view.
2
Identify the two positions
Locate where the Ash'ari position is stated and where the Mu'tazilite position begins.
Ash'aris deny, Mu'tazilites affirm.
3
Understand the Mu'tazilite distinction
The Mu'tazilites say that 'ajz (inability) opposes power, while man' (prevention) opposes the object of power (maqdur). Note the examples given.
This distinction is key to their argument.
4
Analyze the first argument
The Mu'tazilites appeal to intuition: we know the difference between a paralyzed person and a restrained healthy person. That difference must lie in capacity.
Think about why this argument seems compelling.
