Al-Amidi's Critique and the Role of Different Directions
Al-Amidi critiques the distinction and the passage explains the mention of different directions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Amidi's objection that the distinction between separate and contiguous loci is arbitrary, and why the mention of 'different directions' is significant.

Core Idea
Al-Amidi argues that the generation of movements in both cases is via a single act, making the ruling arbitrary.

After presenting the main argument, Al-Iji includes a critique by Al-Amidi, who was a key figure in kalam. Al-Amidi points out that both separate and contiguous movements are generated by a single direct act (like a hand strike), so the difference in ruling is arbitrary. Additionally, the text discusses why 'different directions' is specified—whether to emphasize the oddity or to indicate a limit. This lesson unpacks these nuances.

Guided Path
1
Read Al-Amidi's comment
Identify his main objection: the branch is agreed upon but the distinction is arbitrary because both cases involve generation via a single act.
Al-Amidi says the proof using the gnat/mountain is based on a false premise.
2
Understand the two interpretations of 'different directions'
The passage gives two possible reasons for specifying different directions: either as an a fortiori argument (if it works for different directions, it works for one) or because they don't permit a single power to generate movements to a single direction in separate loci.
This clarifies that the argument is not just about many movements but about the complexity of directions.
3
Synthesize the debate
Notice that Al-Amidi's critique challenges the internal consistency of the theory of generation, forcing us to think about what is truly necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع ( الثالث : ) وهو مبني على تأثير القدرة الحادثة والتوليد أيضاً ( قالوا : القدرة الواحدة قد تولد في محال متفرقة حركات ) متعددة (إلى جهات مختلفة) فيجوز أن يحرك الشخص بقدرة واحدة جزءاً إلى جهة وجزءاً آخر إلى جهة أخرى وجزءاً ثالثاً إلى جهة ثالثة وهكذا بأن يضرب مثلاً يده عليها دفعة فتتفرق في تلك الجهات ( وأما في محال مجتمعة كأجزاء متلاصقة ( فلا يجوز أن تولد القدرة الواحدة فيها حركات متعددة بأن تتحرك معا إلى جهة واحدة ( بل يجتمع على عشرة أجزاء مجتمعة ) متلاصقة ) عشرة أجزاء من القدرة فالقدرة على تحريك كل جزء ) تلك العشرة المجتمعة ( غير القدرة على تحريك ( الجزء (الآخر) فيكون هناك عشر قدر إزاء عشرة أجزاء، وبالجملة يجب أن يكون عدد القدرة القائمة بالقادر على التحريك مساوياً لعدد الأجزاء المجتمعة ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم تكن القدرة على تحريك جزء غير القدرة على تحريك الجزء الآخر، بل جاز أن يكون القدرة على تحريك جزء قدرة على تحريك جزئين ( لكان ) أي تلك القدرة وذكرها بتأويل التمكن ) قدرة على تحريك الأجزاء بالغة ما بلغت ( إذ ليس عدد أولى من عدد فيلزم أن تقدر البقة على تحريك الجبل وهو باطل بالضرورة وقد عرفت بطلان عدم الأولوية. قال الآمدي : هذا الفرع مما اتفق عليه القائلون بالتوليد، وهو من قبيل من قوله : ( مختلقة) قيد اتفاقي فإن الأجزاء المتفرقة بعد الضرب يتحرك كل منها إلى جهة من الجهات الاعتبارية، وكذا وحدة الجهة المتلاصقة فإن تحركهما لتلاصقهما يكون إلى جهة واحدة. قوله : (قال الآمدي الخ) يعني أن كل واحد من الأجزاء واقعة بطريق التوليد بتوسط فعل واحد مباشر في الصورتين، وهو كضرب اليد مثلاً، فالقول بوقوعها في حال التفرق بقدرة واحدة وامتناعها حال الاجتماع تحكم والفرق الذي ذكروه من لزوم قدرة البقة على تحريك الجبل باطل لابتنائه على مقدمة باطلة . قوله : ( إلى جهات مختلفة) قيل : إنما خص الجهات المختلفة بالذكر لأن صدور الحركات إلى جهات مختلفة من القدرة الواحدة أبعد من صدورها إلى جهة واحدة لأنها أثر واحد فنبه على ذلك بطريق الأولى وقيل : بل لأنهم لا يجوزون توليد القدرة الواحدة حركات المحال تجویزهم المتفرقة إلى جهة واحدة .
The third branch: and it is built on the effect of the originated power and generation as well. They said: The single power may generate in separate loci multiple movements to different directions. So it is permissible for a person to move with a single power a part to a direction, another part to another direction, a third part to a third direction, and so on, for example by striking his hand upon them all at once so they scatter in those directions. But in contiguous loci, like adjacent parts, it is not permissible for a single power to generate multiple movements in them, whether they move together to a single direction. Rather, for ten adjacent parts, ten parts of power come together. So the power to move each of those ten contiguous parts is not the power to move the other part, so there are ten powers corresponding to ten parts. In general, the number of power subsisting in the capable agent for moving must equal the number of contiguous parts. Otherwise—i.e., if the power to move a part were not other than the power to move another part, but rather it were permissible that the power to move a part be a power to move two parts—then that power, mentioning it by the interpretation of capability, would be a power to move parts however many they be, since no number is more fitting than another. Then it would follow that a gnat [or soul?] would have power to move a mountain, which is necessarily false, and you have known the falsity of absence of priority. Al-Amidi said: This branch is among what those who affirm generation have agreed upon, and it is of the kind of... He said: (different) as an accidental qualification, for the separate parts after the strike each move to a direction of the conventional directions; similarly the unity of direction of contiguous parts, for their movement due to their adjacency is to one direction. He said: (Al-Amidi said etc.) meaning that each of the parts occurs by way of generation through the mediation of a single direct act in both cases, like the striking of the hand for example. So the statement that they occur in the state of separation by a single power and their impossibility in the state of conjunction is arbitrary. And the difference they mentioned—the necessity of a gnat's power to move a mountain—is false because it is based on a false premise. He said: (to different directions) It is said that he specified different directions because the occurrence of movements to different directions from a single power is more remote than their occurrence to one direction because it is a single effect, so he hinted at that in an a fortiori way. And it is said that it is because they do not permit a single power to generate movements of loci that they permit separate to a single direction.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents the third branch, which builds on the theory of generation (tawallud) and the effect of originated power. It is an argument about the number of powers required for moving parts. First, the author states that a single power can generate multiple movements in separate loci (e.g., striking a hand on multiple objects causing them to scatter in different directions). This is allowed. However, for contiguous loci (adjacent parts), a single power cannot generate multiple movements. Instead, a separate power is needed for each contiguous part, so that the number of powers equals the number of parts. The reasoning is that if a single power could move two contiguous parts, then it could move any number of parts, leading to the absurd conclusion that a gnat could move a mountain. This is because there is no reason to prioritize one number over another (lack of 'firstness'). Al-Amidi then comments that this branch is agreed upon by proponents of generation, but he critiques it as arbitrary: since each part's movement occurs by generation through a single direct act (like a hand strike) in both cases, there is no principled difference. The distinction of different directions is noted: mentioning different directions emphasizes the oddity of multiple movements from one power, but some say it is because they do not permit a single power to generate movements to a single direction in separate loci.

الآمدي
توليد
قيد اتفاقي
جهات مختلفة
بطريق الأولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Amidi's critique is merely a footnote and does not affect the main argument.
Correction: Al-Amidi's critique challenges the coherence of the main argument, indicating that the theory of generation had internal disagreements.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Amidi, why is the distinction between separate and contiguous loci arbitrary?
A) Because separate loci can never have multiple movements.
B) Because both cases involve generation via a single direct act.
C) Because the proof using the gnat is correct.
D) Because contiguous loci cannot move at all.

Key Takeaway
Al-Amidi's critique reveals an internal tension in the theory of generation: the same process of generation is at work in both separate and contiguous loci, yet the ruling differs, calling for further scrutiny.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you confidently held a judgment about someone that was actually based on an arbitrary distinction—like saying one situation is okay but another identical one is not? How would you feel if someone showed you that your reasoning relied on a false premise?
?
Al-Amidi's critique reveals how even accepted theories can contain unexamined contradictions. What is one area of your own life (a habit, belief, or relationship) where you might be making an exception that does not logically hold? What would it cost you to admit it?
?
The proponents used the 'gnat moving a mountain' example to defend their view, but it was based on a false premise. In what way do you cling to an argument that sounds convincing but collapses under scrutiny? Will you examine that premise today?

Single Power: Separate vs. Contiguous Loci
Presents the argument that a single power generates movements in separate but not contiguous parts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that a single power can generate movements in separate loci but not in contiguous loci, and why the number of powers must equal the number of contiguous parts.

Core Idea
A single power can separate, but not unite, movements in adjacent parts.

This passage presents a key distinction in the kalām theory of generation (tawlīd). The proponents argue that a single power may generate diverse movements in separate parts, but when parts are contiguous, each requires its own power. The reasoning is based on a reductio ad absurdum: if one power could move two contiguous parts, it could move any number, leading to absurd consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the overall argument. Note the distinction between separate (mutaferriqa) and contiguous (mujtami'a) loci.
The passage uses technical terms like 'توليد' (generation) and 'قدرة واحدة' (single power).
2
Distinguish the two cases
Identify how the argument treats separate loci (allowed) versus contiguous loci (not allowed). For separate loci, a single strike can move parts in different directions.
The example of striking the hand on parts illustrates generation in separate loci.
3
Examine the reductio argument
Understand the reductio: if one power could move two contiguous parts, then since no number is more appropriate, it could move any number, even a mountain, which is false. This proves the necessity of distinct powers.
The reductio relies on the principle of 'no priority' (la awlawiyya).
4
Conclude the necessity
The conclusion is that the number of powers must equal the number of contiguous parts. Reflect on why this is a logical, not physical, necessity.
The argument is meant to show that the alternative leads to absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الثالث: وهو مبني على تأثير القدرة الحادثة والتوليد أيضاً. قالوا: القدرة الواحدة قد تولد في محال متفرقة حركات متعددة إلى جهات مختلفة فيجوز أن يحرك الشخص بقدرة واحدة جزءاً إلى جهة وجزءاً آخر إلى جهة أخرى وجزءاً ثالثاً إلى جهة ثالثة وهكذا بأن يضرب مثلاً يده عليها دفعة فتتفرق في تلك الجهات. وأما في محال مجتمعة كأجزاء متلاصقة فلا يجوز أن تولد القدرة الواحدة فيها حركات متعددة بأن تتحرك معا إلى جهة واحدة بل يجتمع على عشرة أجزاء مجتمعة متلاصقة عشرة أجزاء من القدرة فالقدرة على تحريك كل جزء من تلك العشرة المجتمعة غير القدرة على تحريك الجزء الآخر فيكون هناك عشر قدر إزاء عشرة أجزاء. وبالجملة يجب أن يكون عدد القدرة القائمة بالقادر على التحريك مساوياً لعدد الأجزاء المجتمعة. وإلا – أي وإن لم تكن القدرة على تحريك جزء غير القدرة على تحريك الجزء الآخر، بل جاز أن تكون القدرة على تحريك جزء قدرة على تحريك جزئين – لكان – أي تلك القدرة – قدرة على تحريك الأجزاء بالغة ما بلغت إذ ليس عدد أولى من عدد فيلزم أن تقدر البقة على تحريك الجبل وهو باطل بالضرورة وقد عرفت بطلان عدم الأولوية.
The third branch: It is based on the effect of the temporal power and generation as well. They said: The single power may generate in separate loci multiple movements towards different directions, so it is permissible for a person to move with one power a part towards a direction and another part towards another direction and a third part towards a third direction, and so on, for example by striking his hand on them at once so they scatter in those directions. As for in contiguous loci like adjacent parts, it is not permissible for the single power to generate in them multiple movements such that they move together towards one direction. Rather, ten parts of power come together for ten adjacent contiguous parts, so the power to move each part of those ten contiguous parts is not the power to move the other part, so there are ten powers corresponding to ten parts. In sum, the number of powers subsisting in the agent of movement must equal the number of contiguous parts. Otherwise – i.e., if the power to move one part were not other than the power to move the other part, but rather it were permissible for the power to move one part to be a power to move two parts – then that power would be a power to move the parts however many they are, since no number is more appropriate than another number, and thus it would follow that a flea is able to move a mountain, which is necessarily false, and you have already known the falsity of lacking priority.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of the third branch (al-farʿ al-thālith) concerning the generation (tawlīd) of movements by a single power. The author first states that this branch is built upon the effect of temporal power and generation. The proponents (likely the Muʿtazilites who accept generation) claim that a single power can generate multiple movements in separate loci (e.g., different parts of a body moving in different directions after a single strike), but it cannot generate multiple movements in contiguous loci (adjacent parts). In the case of contiguous parts, they argue that each part requires its own distinct power, so the number of powers must equal the number of parts. The proof is a reductio ad absurdum: if one power could move two contiguous parts, then since no number is more appropriate than another, that same power could move any number of parts, even a mountain, which is absurd. Therefore, the distinction between separate and contiguous loci is necessary to avoid the absurd consequence.

التوليد
المحال المتفرقة
المحال المجتمعة
الجزء
الحركة
عدد القدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is based on physical impossibility (e.g., strength limits).
Correction: The argument is logical: it uses the principle of no priority to show that allowing one power for two contiguous parts would entail the power to move any number, which is absurd.

Checkpoint
What is the main logical principle used in the reductio to show that one power cannot move two contiguous parts?
A) The law of non-contradiction.
B) The principle of no priority (la awlawiyya).
C) The principle of sufficient reason.
D) The principle of parsimony.

Key Takeaway
The argument demonstrates that logical necessity forces distinct powers for adjacent parts, preventing absurd consequences like a flea moving a mountain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If a single created power cannot move two adjacent parts without absurdity, how often do you assume your own power is sufficient for multiple adjacent responsibilities? Name one area of your life where you are relying on your own strength rather than Allah's, and commit to turning to Him today.
?
The reductio argument uses a flea moving a mountain to show impossibility. What 'mountain' are you trying to move with your own 'flea-like' power? Write down one burden you are carrying alone that you need to entrust to Allah's infinite power.
?
This lesson distinguishes between separate and contiguous loci. Where in your spiritual life have you blurred that distinction—treating separate blessings as if they require your own power, or treating adjacent difficulties as if they are one big problem? How can this understanding change your reliance on Allah?

Al-Amidi's Critique: Arbitrariness of the Generation Argument
Al-Amidi criticizes the distinction as arbitrary and presents alternative explanations.

Learning Objective
Understand Al-Amidi's critique that the Mu'tazilite distinction between separate and contiguous parts is arbitrary, based on empirical observation rather than rational necessity.

Core Idea
Empirical observation does not prove rational necessity.

In this passage, Al-Amidi launches a sharp critique against the Mu'tazilite argument about a single power moving separate parts but not contiguous ones. He labels their reasoning as 'cold arbitraries' and points out that even leading Mu'tazilites like Abu Hashim admit they have no rational explanation—only an empirical observation that separate parts are easier to move. Al-Amidi then shows that this observation does not entail the necessity of multiple powers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the rhythm of Al-Amidi's critique come through.
Notice the strong language: 'cold arbitraries'.
2
Identify the core problem
Al-Amidi asks why a single power can move separate parts but not contiguous ones, even though no new heaviness or increase occurs. This is the puzzle at the heart of the Mu'tazilite argument.
3
Consider Abu Hashim's admission
Abu Hashim and other leading Mu'tazilites admit they have no rational reason—only an empirical observation: separate parts are easier to move. Mark where this appears in the text.
This is a crucial concession.
4
Analyze Al-Amidi's alternatives
Al-Amidi offers two alternative explanations: divine custom (God creates power only in separation) or the possibility of an additional power in joining without requiring equal number. Notice how these undermine the necessity of multiple powers.
These are not claims of fact but possibilities that show the argument does not prove rational necessity.
5
Conclude the lesson's insight
Reflect: why does this critique matter for the broader debate on causality? It shows that a key Mu'tazilite distinction rests on empirical observation, not logical necessity.
This connects to the larger theme of whether secondary causality is rationally necessary or merely customary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الآمدي : هذا الفرع مما اتفق عليه القائلون بالتوليد، وهو من قبيل تحكماتهم الباردة ودعاويهم الجامدة، فإنه إذا قيل لهم : لِمَ كانت القدرة الواحدة تحرك الأجزاء المتفرقة وتوجب في كل واحد منها حركة ويمتنع عليها ذلك عند انضمام الأجزاء مع أنه لم يحدث بالانضمام ثقل ولا زيادة في الأجزاء، بل لا فارق هناك سوى الاجتما، والافتراق ؟ لم يجدو إلى الفرق سبيلاً، ولذلك قال أبو هاشم، وغيره من فضلاء المعتزلة : لا ندري لذلك سبباً غير أنا وجدنا أن ما يسهل علينا تحريكه عند الافتراق يعسر علينا عند ذلك الاجتماع، وهذا الذي قالوه وإن كان حقاً إلا أنه لا يدل على وجوب اجتماع قدر موازية لأعداد الأجزاء المتلاصقة ولا على أن يكون هناك حركات بعدد الأجزاء لجواز أن يقال جري عادته تعالى بخلق القدرة على التحريك حال الافتراق دون الاجتماع، وأن يقال أيضاً : جاز التحريك في المجتمعة على وجود قدرة أخرى منضمة إلى الأولى من غير أن يكون عدد القدر بعدد الأجزاء، ولا محيص لهم عن ذلك. وأما الجبائي فإنه قال : انضمام الأجزاء مانع من التحريك ألا ترى أنا نجد القادر على المشي يمتنع عليه المشي بالربط والتقييد وليس ذلك إلا بسبب الاجتماع. قوله: (ما يسهل علينا) بحيث لا يقع بقرينة قوله : بخلق التحريك حال التفرق دون الاجتماع، وفيه بحث لأن الغرض في بعض الصور مسلم والمستدل معترف بعدم خلق القدرة لكن الكلام فيما إذا وقعت التحريك حال الافتراق والاجتماع بطريق التوليد من فعل واحد مباشر، ففي حال الافتراق واقع بقدرة واحدة وفي حال الاجتماع بقدرة متعددة، وبهذا ظهر بطلان الفرق بما ذكره الجبائي من أن الاجتماع مانع التحريك، وأن ما ذكره ليس من تتمة الفرع الثالث، إذ معناه أنه بعد وقوع حركة الأجزاء في الحالين، بل هو بقدرة واحدة أو بقدرة متعددة، بل هو فرع بانفرادها، وإن جعل الآمدي من ثمة قدرة وصدور المصنف، فإن نظره أدق. قوله : ( من غير أن يكون إلخ ) وما ذكرته من أنه ليس عدد أولى من عدد فباطل. قوله : (وهو من قبيل تحكماتهم الباردة وفيه أيضاً مناقضة أصلهم من وجوب تعلق القدرة الواحدة الحادثة بجميع أجناس مقدورات المخلوق . قوله : ( لم يجدو إلى الفرق سبيلاً فإن قلت لهم أن يقولوا : توليد القدرة ابتداء أقوى منه بواسطة أو أكثر وفي صورة الاجتماع التحريك للجزء الذي تمسه اليد بلا واسطة، ولما بعده بواسطة أو أكثر. قلت يمكن أن تمس اليد جميع الأجزاء في صورة الاجتماع، كما إذا فرضنا سطحاً جوهرياً مركباً من الجواهر الفردة ويوضح على الكف ويرفع وأن لا يمس في صورة التفريق إلا بعض الأجزاء وهو ظاهر، فلا يجدي الفرق المذكور في جميع المواضع.
Al-Amidi said: This branch is something agreed upon by the proponents of generation, and it is among their cold arbitraries and rigid claims. For if it is said to them: Why does a single power move the separate parts and cause in each one of them a movement, but it is prevented from doing so when the parts are joined, even though joining does not produce heaviness or increase in the parts, and there is no difference except aggregation and separation? They would not find a way to differentiate. Therefore Abu Hashim and other virtuous Mu'tazilites said: We do not know a reason for that except that we find that what is easy for us to move when separated becomes difficult for us when joined. And what they said, although true, does not prove the necessity of gathering an amount of power equivalent to the number of contiguous parts, nor that there are movements equal to the number of parts, because it is possible to say: His custom has proceeded to create the power for movement in the state of separation but not in the state of joining, and it could also be said: movement in contiguous parts is permissible with the existence of another power joined to the first, without the number of powers being equal to the number of parts. And they have no escape from that. As for al-Jubbai, he said: The joining of parts is a preventer of movement; do you not see that we find the one able to walk is prevented from walking when bound and tied, and that is only due to joining. His statement: (what is easy for us) such that it does not occur, by the context of his statement: by creating movement in separation not in joining. And there is discussion because the purpose in some cases is conceded and the arguer acknowledges the non-creation of power, but the discussion is about when movement occurs in separation and joining by way of generation from a single direct act. So in the state of separation it occurs with a single power, and in the state of joining with multiple powers. And by this the falsity of the distinction mentioned by al-Jubbai – that joining prevents movement – is made clear, and that what he mentioned is not a completion of the third branch, for its meaning is after the occurrence of movement of the parts in both states, but it is with a single power or multiple powers; rather it is a branch by itself, even if al-Amidi from there posited power and the author's statements, for his view is more precise. His statement: (without it being etc.) and what you mentioned that no number is more appropriate than another is false. His statement: (and it is among their cold arbitraries) and in it is also a contradiction of their principle of the necessity of the single temporal power's attachment to all kinds of the creature's possible acts. His statement: (they do not find a way to differentiate) for if you say to them that they may say: the generation of power initially is stronger than through one intermediary or more, and in the case of joining, the movement of the part touched by the hand is without intermediary, and what is after it is through one or more intermediaries. I say: It is possible that the hand touches all parts in the case of joining, as if we suppose a substantial surface composed of individual atoms and it is placed on the palm and lifted, and that in the case of separation it touches only some parts – that is obvious – so the mentioned distinction is not useful in all cases.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage contains al-Amidi's critique of the argument presented in the previous lesson. He labels the argument as arbitrary ('cold arbitraries'). He points out that when asked why a single power can move separate parts but not contiguous ones, the proponents have no real answer except an appeal to experience, as Abu Hashim and other leading Mu'tazilites admitted: they simply observe that separate parts are easier to move. Al-Amidi argues that this empirical observation does not prove the necessity of distinct powers for each contiguous part; one could instead appeal to divine custom (that God creates power only in separation) or the possibility of an additional power in the case of joining without requiring equality of number. Then al-Jubbai's objection is presented: joining itself is a preventer (maniʿ) of movement, analogous to being bound. The text then offers a rebuttal: the discussion concerns generation from a single direct act, and in separation it occurs with one power, in joining with multiple powers, falsifying al-Jubbai's claim. Finally, a further objection is considered and refuted: the distinction based on direct vs. indirect contact fails because the hand can touch all parts in the case of joining. Thus the critique is thorough, showing the weakness of the original argument.

Correction: Al-Amidi does not reject generation itself; he only criticizes this specific distinction as arbitrary and not rationally necessary.

Al-Jubbai's Prevention Argument and Its Rebuttal
Al-Jubbai's prevention argument and subsequent rebuttals including refutation of contact distinction.

Learning Objective
Understand Al-Jubbai's prevention argument and the subsequent rebuttal that the distinction fails because generation from a single act requires multiple powers only in the case of joining.

Core Idea
Joining as a preventer is refuted by the nature of generation.

This lesson continues the critique by examining Al-Jubbai's argument that joining itself prevents movement, like binding prevents walking. The text provides a rebuttal: the discussion concerns generation from a single direct act, and in separation it occurs with one power, in joining with multiple powers, thus falsifying Al-Jubbai's claim. Additionally, a further objection based on direct vs. indirect contact is refuted by the possibility of hand touching all parts in joining.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note that it begins with Al-Jubbai's objection and then presents a rebuttal.
Pay attention to the transitions: 'قوله:' indicates a quote being discussed.
2
Understand Al-Jubbai's prevention analogy
Al-Jubbai claims that joining is a preventer (maniʿ) of movement, analogous to being bound or tied. Identify this claim and note how it attempts to explain the distinction.
The analogy is based on everyday experience: a bound person cannot walk.
3
Analyze the rebuttal
The rebuttal points out that the discussion is about generation from a single direct act: in separation, a single power generates movement; in joining, multiple powers are needed. This undermines Al-Jubbai's claim that joining itself prevents movement.
The rebuttal also argues that Al-Jubbai's point is not even part of the original third branch, but a separate issue.
4
Examine the refutation of the contact-based distinction
A further objection suggests that generation is stronger initially and weaker through intermediaries, implying that in joining, only the touched part moves directly. The text refutes this by noting that the hand can touch all parts in joining (e.g., a surface placed on the palm).
This shows that the distinction based on direct vs. indirect contact does not hold universally.
5
Conclude the lesson's insight
Reflect: how do these rebuttals strengthen Al-Amidi's overall critique? They show that neither Al-Jubbai's prevention argument nor a contact-based explanation can salvage the original distinction.

قال الآمدي : هذا الفرع مما اتفق عليه القائلون بالتوليد، وهو من قبيل تحكماتهم الباردة ودعاويهم الجامدة، فإنه إذا قيل لهم : لِمَ كانت القدرة الواحدة تحرك الأجزاء المتفرقة وتوجب في كل واحد منها حركة ويمتنع عليها ذلك عند انضمام الأجزاء مع أنه لم يحدث بالانضمام ثقل ولا زيادة في الأجزاء، بل لا فارق هناك سوى الاجتما، والافتراق ؟ لم يجدو إلى الفرق سبيلاً، ولذلك قال أبو هاشم، وغيره من فضلاء المعتزلة : لا ندري لذلك سبباً غير أنا وجدنا أن ما يسهل علينا تحريكه عند الافتراق يعسر علينا عند ذلك الاجتماع، وهذا الذي قالوه وإن كان حقاً إلا أنه لا يدل على وجوب اجتماع قدر موازية لأعداد الأجزاء المتلاصقة ولا على أن يكون هناك حركات بعدد الأجزاء لجواز أن يقال جري عادته تعالى بخلق القدرة على التحريك حال الافتراق دون الاجتماع، وأن يقال أيضاً : جاز التحريك في المجتمعة على وجود قدرة أخرى منضمة إلى الأولى من غير أن يكون عدد القدر بعدد الأجزاء، ولا محيص لهم عن ذلك. وأما الجبائي فإنه قال : انضمام الأجزاء مانع من التحريك ألا ترى أنا نجد القادر على المشي يمتنع عليه المشي بالربط والتقييد وليس ذلك إلا بسبب الاجتماع. قوله: (ما يسهل علينا) بحيث لا يقع بقرينة قوله : بخلق التحريك حال التفرق دون الاجتماع، وفيه بحث لأن الغرض في بعض الصور مسلم والمستدل معترف بعدم خلق القدرة لكن الكلام فيما إذا وقعت التحريك حال الافتراق والاجتماع بطريق التوليد من فعل واحد مباشر، ففي حال الافتراق واقع بقدرة واحدة وفي حال الاجتماع بقدرة متعددة، وبهذا ظهر بطلان الفرق بما ذكره الجبائي من أن الاجتماع مانع التحريك، وأن ما ذكره ليس من تتمة الفرع الثالث، إذ معناه أنه بعد وقوع حركة الأجزاء في الحالين، بل هو بقدرة واحدة أو بقدرة متعددة، بل هو فرع بانفرادها، وإن جعل الآمدي من ثمة قدرة وصدور المصنف، فإن نظره أدق. قوله : ( من غير أن يكون إلخ ) وما ذكرته من أنه ليس عدد أولى من عدد فباطل. قوله : (وهو من قبيل تحكماتهم الباردة وفيه أيضاً مناقضة أصلهم من وجوب تعلق القدرة الواحدة الحادثة بجميع أجناس مقدورات المخلوق . قوله : ( لم يجدو إلى الفرق سبيلاً فإن قلت لهم أن يقولوا : توليد القدرة ابتداء أقوى منه بواسطة أو أكثر وفي صورة الاجتماع التحريك للجزء الذي تمسه اليد بلا واسطة، ولما بعده بواسطة أو أكثر. قلت يمكن أن تمس اليد جميع الأجزاء في صورة الاجتماع، كما إذا فرضنا سطحاً جوهرياً مركباً من الجواهر الفردة ويوضح على الكف ويرفع وأن لا يمس في صورة التفريق إلا بعض الأجزاء وهو ظاهر، فلا يجدي الفرق المذكور في جميع المواضع.
Al-Amidi said: This branch is something agreed upon by the proponents of generation, and it is among their cold arbitraries and rigid claims. For if it is said to them: Why does a single power move the separate parts and cause in each one of them a movement, but it is prevented from doing so when the parts are joined, even though joining does not produce heaviness or increase in the parts, and there is no difference except aggregation and separation? They would not find a way to differentiate. Therefore Abu Hashim and other virtuous Mu'tazilites said: We do not know a reason for that except that we find that what is easy for us to move when separated becomes difficult for us when joined. And what they said, although true, does not prove the necessity of gathering an amount of power equivalent to the number of contiguous parts, nor that there are movements equal to the number of parts, because it is possible to say: His custom has proceeded to create the power for movement in the state of separation but not in the state of joining, and it could also be said: movement in contiguous parts is permissible with the existence of another power joined to the first, without the number of powers being equal to the number of parts. And they have no escape from that. As for al-Jubbai, he said: The joining of parts is a preventer of movement; do you not see that we find the one able to walk is prevented from walking when bound and tied, and that is only due to joining. His statement: (what is easy for us) such that it does not occur, by the context of his statement: by creating movement in separation not in joining. And there is discussion because the purpose in some cases is conceded and the arguer acknowledges the non-creation of power, but the discussion is about when movement occurs in separation and joining by way of generation from a single direct act. So in the state of separation it occurs with a single power, and in the state of joining with multiple powers. And by this the falsity of the distinction mentioned by al-Jubbai – that joining prevents movement – is made clear, and that what he mentioned is not a completion of the third branch, for its meaning is after the occurrence of movement of the parts in both states, but it is with a single power or multiple powers; rather it is a branch by itself, even if al-Amidi from there posited power and the author's statements, for his view is more precise. His statement: (without it being etc.) and what you mentioned that no number is more appropriate than another is false. His statement: (and it is among their cold arbitraries) and in it is also a contradiction of their principle of the necessity of the single temporal power's attachment to all kinds of the creature's possible acts. His statement: (they do not find a way to differentiate) for if you say to them that they may say: the generation of power initially is stronger than through one intermediary or more, and in the case of joining, the movement of the part touched by the hand is without intermediary, and what is after it is through one or more intermediaries. The Problem of Separate vs. Aggregated Parts and Abu Hashim's Response
The problem posed to philosophers and Abu Hashim's empirical response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalām problem of why a single power can move separate parts but not aggregated parts, and the empirical response of Abu Hashim and its limitations.

Core Idea
Difficulty of moving aggregated parts does not prove multiple powers — divine custom suffices.

The passage presents a challenge to the philosophers: why can a single power move separate parts but not when they are aggregated, despite no physical change? Abu Hashim and other Mu'tazilites respond that they only know from experience that separate parts are easier to move. The commentator then critiques this response, showing that it does not logically necessitate multiple powers — it could be a matter of divine custom or an additional power.

Guided Path
1
Read the problem
Read the opening lines where the challenge is posed: why does aggregation prevent movement without any increase in weight?
Notice that the philosophers have no answer.
2
Understand Abu Hashim's response
Abu Hashim admits ignorance of the cause, only citing empirical ease of moving separate parts.
3
Identify the critique
The commentator argues that this observation does not prove the need for multiple powers; divine custom is a possible explanation.
This shows the limits of empirical reasoning in kalām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع - المقصد العاشر : قول الحكماء حول النوم والقدرة تحكماتهم الباردة ودعاويهم الجامدة، فإنه إذا قيل لهم : لِمَ كانت القدرة الواحدة تحرك الأجزاء المتفرقة وتوجب في كل واحد منها حركة ويمتنع عليها ذلك عند انضمام الأجزاء مع أنه لم يحدث بالانضمام ثقل ولا زيادة في الأجزاء، بل لا فارق هناك سوى الاجتما، والافتراق ؟ لم يجدو إلى الفرق سبيلاً، ولذلك قال أبو هاشم، وغيره من فضلاء المعتزلة : لا ندري لذلك سبباً غير أنا وجدنا أن ما يسهل علينا تحريكه عند الافتراق يعسر علينا عند ذلك الاجتماع، وهذا الذي قالوه وإن كان حقاً إلا أنه لا يدل على وجوب اجتماع قدر موازية لأعداد الأجزاء المتلاصقة ولا على أن يكون هناك حركات بعدد الأجزاء لجواز أن يقال جري عادته تعالى بخلق القدرة على التحريك حال الافتراق دون الاجتماع، وأن يقال أيضاً : جاز التحريك في المجتمعة على وجود قدرة أخرى منضمة إلى الأولى من غير أن يكون عدد القدر بعدد الأجزاء، ولا محيص لهم عن ذلك، وأما الجبائي فإنه قال : انضمام الأجزاء مانع من التحريك ألا ترى أنا نجد القادر على المشي يمتنع عليه المشي بالربط والتقييد وليس ذلك إلا بسبب انضمام أجزاء القيد إلى رجليه وهو مبني على أصله في جواز منع القادر، وقد بان بطلانه وإن سلمنا صحة المنع فلا نسلم صحة التعليل بانضمام أجزاء القيد إلى رجليه، بل جاز أن يكون المنع لمعنى مختص بصورة القيد ولا وجود له فيما نحن فيه من الأجزاء المجتمعة وكيف لا والفرق واقع بينهما من جهة أن مانع القيد لا يزول وإن تضاعفت القدرة بخلاف الأجزاء المجتمعة فإنه قال : بزوال المانع بتقديران يوجد قدر موازية لعدد الأجزاء المنضمة، ومما نقلناه تبين أن كلام الجبائي من الفرع الثالث، كما هو المناسب لكن الموجود في أكثر نسخ الكتاب هكذا ( الرابع ) أي من الفروع : ( قال الجبائي : الاجتماع يمنع التحريك كالقيد ( فإنه مانع عن المشي لمن هو قادر عليه (وهو) أي كون القيد مانعا عن الفعل ( فرع أن المعدوم مقدور ) حتى يتصور كون القادر على فعل ممنوعاً منه، إذ لا مجال للمنع بالقياس إلى الفعل الموجود لكنا بينا بطلان كون المعدوم مقدورا بما ثبت من وجوب كون القدرة مع الفعل لا قبله (وبه ) أي بكون الاجتماع مانعا عن التحريك (منع) الجبائي (كون القادر على حمل مئة من قادراً على حمل المئة الأخرى معها وحكم بأنه ليس قادراً على حملها، وفيه بحث لأن كون الاجتماع مانعاً من الفعل يقتضي كون ذلك القادر قادراً على حمل الأخرى ممنوعاً منه لا كونه غير قادر عليه .
The fourth type - the tenth topic: The saying of the philosophers concerning sleep and power, their cold speculations and rigid claims. For if it is said to them: Why does the single power move the separate parts and necessitate in each one of them a movement, but it is impossible for it to do so when the parts are joined, even though joining does not produce heaviness or increase in the parts, and there is no difference there except togetherness and separation? They find no way to differentiate. Therefore Abu Hashim and other distinguished Mu'tazilites said: We know no cause for that except that we find that what is easy for us to move when separate becomes difficult for us when they are together. This statement of theirs, though true, does not prove the necessity of a power equal to the number of contiguous parts nor that there are movements equal to the number of parts, because it is possible to say that His custom, exalted be He, runs to create power for movement in the state of separation but not in the state of togetherness, and also to say: Movement is possible in the aggregated parts by the existence of another power joined to the first without the number of powers being equal to the number of parts. They cannot escape that. As for al-Jubbai, he said: The joining of the parts is a preventer of movement; do you not see that we find the one capable of walking is prevented from walking by tying and binding, and that is only due to the joining of the parts of the fetter to his legs. This is based on his principle concerning the permissibility of preventing the capable one, and its falsity has been shown. Even if we grant the validity of prevention, we do not grant the validity of the explanation by the joining of the parts of the fetter to his legs; rather, it is possible that the prevention is for a meaning specific to the form of the fetter which does not exist in the case under discussion of the aggregated parts. How not, and the difference between them is clear in that the preventer of the fetter does not disappear even if power is multiplied, unlike the aggregated parts, for he said: The preventer disappears if a power equal to the number of joined parts exists. From what we have transmitted, it becomes clear that al-Jubbai's statement belongs to the third branch, as is appropriate, but what is found in most copies of the book is thus: (The fourth) of the branches: (Al-Jubbai said: Togetherness prevents movement like the fetter, for it prevents walking for one who is capable of it. (It) i.e., the fetter being a preventer of the act, is a branch of the non-existent being an object of power, so that it is conceivable that the one capable of an act is prevented from it, for there is no room for prevention with respect to an existing act. But we have shown the falsity of the non-existent being an object of power by what has been established of the necessity that power be with the act, not before it. (And by it) i.e., by togetherness being a preventer of movement, al-Jubbai prevented (the idea that) the one capable of carrying a hundred is capable of carrying the other hundred together with it, and he judged that he is not capable of carrying it. There is discussion here, because togetherness being a preventer of the act implies that that capable one is capable of carrying the other but prevented from it, not that he is not capable of it.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage discusses a kalām argument about the nature of power (qudra) and movement. It begins by posing the problem: why can a single power move separate parts but not aggregated parts, even though aggregation does not add weight or increase the parts? The Mu'tazilite Abu Hashim and others admit they do not know the cause, only that experience shows separate parts are easier to move. The commentator then critiques this, noting that it does not prove the need for multiple powers; it could be a matter of divine custom. Al-Jubbai argues that aggregation is a preventive (māniʿ) like a fetter, but the commentator refutes this by showing that the fetter's prevention is unique and does not apply to aggregated parts in general. The discussion also ties into the branch (farʿ) about whether the non-existent is an object of power (maqdūr), which al-Jubbai uses to support his view, but the commentator rejects that principle. The passage follows the order: problem, response, critique, alternative explanation, and refutation of al-Jubbai's analogy.

تحريك
أجزاء متفرقة
أجزاء مجتمعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The difficulty of moving aggregated parts necessarily proves that multiple powers are required.
Correction: The passage argues that it could be due to divine custom or another power joined, not necessarily multiple powers equal to number of parts.

According to the passage, what did Abu Hashim and other Mu'tazilites say regarding the reason why a single power can move separate parts but not aggregated parts?
A) They knew it was due to increased weight.
B) They admitted they only knew from experience that separate parts are easier.
C) They argued that aggregation creates a preventer.
D) They claimed multiple powers are needed.

Key Takeaway
The problem of aggregated versus separate parts reveals that empirical observation alone cannot establish the necessity of multiple powers; divine custom is a plausible alternative.

Al-Jubbai's Argument and Its Refutation
Al-Jubbai's argument that aggregation prevents movement like a fetter and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jubbai's argument that aggregation prevents movement like a fetter, and the commentator's refutation of both the analogy and the underlying principle.

Core Idea
Al-Jubbai's fetter analogy fails because the prevention is specific and his principle is false.

Al-Jubbai attempts to explain the prevention of movement in aggregated parts by analogy with a fetter that prevents walking. He bases this on his principle that the non-existent is an object of power. The commentator systematically refutes both the analogy and the principle, showing that the fetter's prevention is unique and that the principle itself is false. This lesson clarifies the limits of analogical reasoning in kalām.

Guided Path
1
Understand al-Jubbai's argument
Al-Jubbai claims that aggregation is a preventer (māniʿ) like a fetter, based on the principle that the non-existent can be an object of power.
He uses the example of walking prevented by a fetter.
2
Examine the refutation of the analogy
The commentator argues that the fetter's prevention is specific and does not disappear with increased power, unlike aggregated parts where the preventer would vanish if power is multiplied.
3
Understand the rejection of the principle
The commentator rejects the principle that the non-existent is an object of power, stating that power must be simultaneous with the act.
This is a key kalām doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع - المقصد العاشر : قول الحكماء حول النوم والقدرة تحكماتهم الباردة ودعاويهم الجامدة، فإنه إذا قيل لهم : لِمَ كانت القدرة الواحدة تحرك الأجزاء المتفرقة وتوجب في كل واحد منها حركة ويمتنع عليها ذلك عند انضمام الأجزاء مع أنه لم يحدث بالانضمام ثقل ولا زيادة في الأجزاء، بل لا فارق هناك سوى الاجتما، والافتراق ؟ لم يجدو إلى الفرق سبيلاً، ولذلك قال أبو هاشم، وغيره من فضلاء المعتزلة : لا ندري لذلك سبباً غير أنا وجدنا أن ما يسهل علينا تحريكه عند الافتراق يعسر علينا عند ذلك الاجتماع، وهذا الذي قالوه وإن كان حقاً إلا أنه لا يدل على وجوب اجتماع قدر موازية لأعداد الأجزاء المتلاصقة ولا على أن يكون هناك حركات بعدد الأجزاء لجواز أن يقال جري عادته تعالى بخلق القدرة على التحريك حال الافتراق دون الاجتماع، وأن يقال أيضاً : جاز التحريك في المجتمعة على وجود قدرة أخرى منضمة إلى الأولى من غير أن يكون عدد القدر بعدد الأجزاء، ولا محيص لهم عن ذلك، وأما الجبائي فإنه قال : انضمام الأجزاء مانع من التحريك ألا ترى أنا نجد القادر على المشي يمتنع عليه المشي بالربط والتقييد وليس ذلك إلا بسبب انضمام أجزاء القيد إلى رجليه وهو مبني على أصله في جواز منع القادر، وقد بان بطلانه وإن سلمنا صحة المنع فلا نسلم صحة التعليل بانضمام أجزاء القيد إلى رجليه، بل جاز أن يكون المنع لمعنى مختص بصورة القيد ولا وجود له فيما نحن فيه من الأجزاء المجتمعة وكيف لا والفرق واقع بينهما من جهة أن مانع القيد لا يزول وإن تضاعفت القدرة بخلاف الأجزاء المجتمعة فإنه قال : بزوال المانع بتقديران يوجد قدر موازية لعدد الأجزاء المنضمة، ومما نقلناه تبين أن كلام الجبائي من الفرع الثالث، كما هو المناسب لكن الموجود في أكثر نسخ الكتاب هكذا ( الرابع ) أي من الفروع : ( قال الجبائي : الاجتماع يمنع التحريك كالقيد ( فإنه مانع عن المشي لمن هو قادر عليه (وهو) أي كون القيد مانعا عن الفعل ( فرع أن المعدوم مقدور ) حتى يتصور كون القادر على فعل ممنوعاً منه، إذ لا مجال للمنع بالقياس إلى الفعل الموجود لكنا بينا بطلان كون المعدوم مقدورا بما ثبت من وجوب كون القدرة مع الفعل لا قبله (وبه ) أي بكون الاجتماع مانعا عن التحريك (منع) الجبائي (كون القادر على حمل مئة من قادراً على حمل المئة الأخرى معها وحكم بأنه ليس قادراً على حملها، وفيه بحث لأن كون الاجتماع مانعاً من الفعل يقتضي كون ذلك القادر قادراً على حمل الأخرى ممنوعاً منه لا كونه غير قادر عليه .
The fourth type - the tenth topic: The saying of the philosophers concerning sleep and power, their cold speculations and rigid claims. For if it is said to them: Why does the single power move the separate parts and necessitate in each one of them a movement, but it is impossible for it to do so when the parts are joined, even though joining does not produce heaviness or increase in the parts, and there is no difference there except togetherness and separation? They find no way to differentiate. Therefore Abu Hashim and other distinguished Mu'tazilites said: We know no cause for that except that we find that what is easy for us to move when separate becomes difficult for us when they are together. This statement of theirs, though true, does not prove the necessity of a power equal to the number of contiguous parts nor that there are movements equal to the number of parts, because it is possible to say that His custom, exalted be He, runs to create power for movement in the state of separation but not in the state of togetherness, and also to say: Movement is possible in the aggregated parts by the existence of another power joined to the first without the number of powers being equal to the number of parts. They cannot escape that. As for al-Jubbai, he said: The joining of the parts is a preventer of movement; do you not see that we find the one capable of walking is prevented from walking by tying and binding, and that is only due to the joining of the parts of the fetter to his legs. This is based on his principle concerning the permissibility of preventing the capable one, and its falsity has been shown. Even if we grant the validity of prevention, we do not grant the validity of the explanation by the joining of the parts of the fetter to his legs; rather, it is possible that the prevention is for a meaning specific to the form of the fetter which does not exist in the case under discussion of the aggregated parts. How not, and the difference between them is clear in that the preventer of the fetter does not disappear even if power is multiplied, unlike the aggregated parts, for he said: The preventer disappears if a power equal to the number of joined parts exists. From what we have transmitted, it becomes clear that al-Jubbai's statement belongs to the third branch, as is appropriate, but what is found in most copies of the book is thus: (The fourth) of the branches: (Al-Jubbai said: Togetherness prevents movement like the fetter, for it prevents walking for one who is capable of it. (It) i.e., the fetter being a preventer of the act, is a branch of the non-existent being an object of power, so that it is conceivable that the one capable of an act is prevented from it, for there is no room for prevention with respect to an existing act. But we have shown the falsity of the non-existent being an object of power by what has been established of the necessity that power be with the act, not before it. (And by it) i.e., by togetherness being a preventer of movement, al-Jubbai prevented (the idea that) the one capable of carrying a hundred is capable of carrying the other hundred together with it, and he judged that he is not capable of carrying it. There is discussion here, because togetherness being a preventer of the act implies that that capable one is capable of carrying the other but prevented from it, not that he is not capable of it.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage discusses a kalām argument about the nature of power (qudra) and movement. It begins by posing the problem: why can a single power move separate parts but not aggregated parts, even though aggregation does not add weight or increase the parts? The Mu'tazilite Abu Hashim and others admit they do not know the cause, only that experience shows separate parts are easier to move. The commentator then critiques this, noting that it does not prove the need for multiple powers; it could be a matter of divine custom. Al-Jubbai argues that aggregation is a preventive (māniʿ) like a fetter, but the commentator refutes this by showing that the fetter's prevention is unique and does not apply to aggregated parts in general. The discussion also ties into the branch (farʿ) about whether the non-existent is an object of power (maqdūr), which al-Jubbai uses to support his view, but the commentator rejects that principle. The passage follows the order: problem, response, critique, alternative explanation, and refutation of al-Jubbai's analogy.

مانع
توليد
معدوم مقدور

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Jubbai's argument that aggregation prevents movement like a fetter is logically sound.
Correction: The commentator shows that the fetter analogy does not hold because the prevention is specific, and the underlying principle that the non-existent is an object of power is false in kalām.

Checkpoint
On what principle does al-Jubbai base his argument that aggregation prevents movement like a fetter?
A) The non-existent is an object of power.
B) Power must be simultaneous with the act.
C) Aggregation increases weight.
D) The preventer disappears with increased power.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubbai's attempt to explain the prevention of movement in aggregated parts by analogy to a fetter fails because the fetter's prevention is unique and his principle that the non-existent is an object of power is rejected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fail in an act of obedience, do you assume you were incapable, or do you consider that you were prevented by something that you could overcome with stronger reliance on Allah? How does this lesson's distinction between prevention and incapacity challenge you to examine your excuses?
?
Al-Jubbai's argument relied on a false principle. What false beliefs about Allah's power or your own capability might you be holding that prevent you from trusting Him fully? What specific step can you take this week to act based on the truth that Allah's power is sufficient?
?
The commentator notes that prevention implies capability, not incapability. Think of a duty you've avoided because you felt 'prevented.' How does this truth from the lesson move you to take responsibility and seek Allah's help instead of using prevention as an excuse?

The Essential Cause of Pain: Tafarruq al-Ittisal and al-Razi's Objection
Philosophers and physicians affirm tafarruq al-ittisal as sole essential cause; al-Razi objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical and medical claim that tafarruq al-ittisal is the sole essential cause of pain and the challenge posed by Imam al-Razi.

Core Idea
Pain's essential cause is contested; tafarruq al-ittisal is not universally accepted.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif presents the view that pain's essential cause is tafarruq al-ittisal (discontinuity of continuity), as held by philosophers and Galen. Examples from heat, cold, colors, tastes, smells, and sounds are given to support this. However, Imam al-Razi objects, suggesting that a sharp cut without immediate pain indicates another cause. The lesson explores this debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how it first states the thesis, then provides examples from various senses, and concludes with an objection.
The passage is cut off at the end; we only have the beginning of al-Razi's objection.
2
Identify the thesis and examples
The thesis is that tafarruq al-ittisal is the sole essential cause. The examples show how different stimuli cause pain through separation (or gathering leading to separation). List the examples by sense.
Note that al-Razi's objection undermines the claim of sole causality.
3
Understand al-Razi's objection
Al-Razi's example: cutting with a sharp knife does not produce immediate pain. This suggests that tafarruq al-ittisal may not be the direct cause, as pain is delayed. Reflect on why this challenges the thesis.
The text is incomplete; the full objection and response are missing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال الحكماء: الألم سببه الذاتي تفرق الاتصال فقط بالتجربة، وهذا مذهب جالينوس. فالحار إنما يوجع ويؤلم لأنه يفرق الاتصال، وكذا البارد يلزمه تفرق الاتصال، لأنه لشدة تكثيفه وجمعه يوجب انجذاب الأجزاء إلى ما يتكاثف إليه ويلزم من ذلك تفرقها مما تنجذب عنه، والأسود الحالك المظلم يؤلم لشدة جمعه، والأبيض اليقق لشدة تفريقه، والمر والحامض من المذوقات يؤلمان لفرط التفريق، والعفص والقابض لفرط التقبيض المستشيع للتفريق، وكذا الحال في المشمومات فبعضها مفرق وبعضها مكثف، والأصوات القوية تؤلم بالتفريق التابع لعنف الحركة الهوائية عند ملاقاة الصماخ. وبالجملة اتفق الأطباء على أن تفرق الاتصال سبب ذاتي للوجع. وأنكره الإمام الرازي، فإن من عقر يده بسكين شديدة الحدة في الغاية لم يحس بالألم إلا [؟].
قوله: (وسبب الذاتي إلخ) أي القريب على ما في شرح المقاصد من أن المراد بالسبب الذاتي ما لا يحتاج إلى سبب يتوسط بينه وبين المسبب.
قوله: (تفرق الاتصال) حاصل الكلام أن الأطباء بعدما اتفقوا على أن كلاً من تفرق الاتصال، وهو المزاج المختلف، يقع سبباً للوجع في الجملة، وأنه لا سبب له سواهما، إما بالاستقراء أو بالاستدلال، وإن كان ضعيفاً وهو أن كمال العضو صحة، وهي بالمزاج المعتدل والهيئة التي بها يتأتى الأفعال على ما يجب، فالمنافي لهذا الكمال يكون مبطلاً، لاعتدال المزاج وهو للمزاج المختلف، أو للهيئة وهو تفرق الاتصال، اختلفوا في أن كلاً منهما سبب بالذات، وإليه ذهب الشيخ، أو أن السبب بالذات هو تفرق الاتصال فقط وسوء المزاج سبب بواسطة تفرق الاتصال، ذهب جالينوس وكثير من الأطباء، أو أن السبب بالذات سوء المزاج فقط، والتفرقة إنما يكون سبباً بواسطته، وإليه ذهب الإمام الرازي وجمع من المتأخرين.
قوله: (فإن من عقر يده إلخ) أجيب بأن قطع العضو سريعاً بآلة في غاية الحدة إن كان من [؟].
Then the philosophers said: Pain's essential cause is only discontinuity of continuity, by experience, and this is the doctrine of Galen. For heat causes pain and hurts only because it disunites continuity; likewise the cold necessarily involves discontinuity of continuity, because due to its intense thickening and gathering it causes the attraction of parts to that which they thicken towards, and necessarily resulting from that is their separation from that from which they are attracted. The intensely black, dark [color] hurts due to its intense gathering, and the intensely white [color] due to its intense separating. The bitter and sour among tastes hurt due to excessive separating, and astringent and contractive [tastes] due to excessive contracting that leads to separation. Similarly in smells: some are separating, some are thickening. Strong sounds hurt by the separation consequent on the violence of air movement when meeting the eardrum. In short, the physicians agreed that discontinuity of continuity is an essential cause of pain. And Imam al-Razi denied it, for one who cuts his hand with a very sharp knife in the extreme does not feel pain except [؟]. His saying: (and the essential cause etc.) i.e., the proximate [cause], as in Sharh al-Maqasid, that what is meant by essential cause is that which does not need a cause mediating between it and the effect. His saying: (discontinuity of continuity) The sum of the discourse is that the physicians, after agreeing that both discontinuity of continuity and the different temperament occur as a cause of pain in general, and that there is no cause for it other than them, either by induction or by reasoning - even if it is weak - that the perfection of the organ is health, which is by the balanced temperament and the form by which actions are performed as they should, so what opposes this perfection would be destructive; the balanced temperament is for the different temperament, or the form is for discontinuity of continuity. They disagreed whether each of them is a cause by essence: to that went the Sheikh; or that the essential cause is only discontinuity of continuity and bad temperament is a cause through the mediation of discontinuity of continuity - that was the view of Galen and many physicians; or that the essential cause is only bad temperament, and discontinuity is only a cause through its mediation - to that went Imam al-Razi and a group of later scholars. His saying: (for one who cuts his hand etc.) It is answered that the cutting of the limb quickly with an extremely sharp tool if it is of [؟].
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents a philosophical and medical discussion on the essential cause of pain. It begins by stating the view of the philosophers and Galen: pain's sole essential cause is tafarruq al-ittisal (discontinuity of continuity). Various examples are given: heat, cold, colors, tastes, smells, and loud sounds all cause pain through separation or excessive gathering leading to separation. The physicians agree on this. However, Imam al-Razi objects: if one cuts his hand with an extremely sharp knife, he does not feel pain immediately? The text is cut off, but the objection is that the pain is delayed, suggesting another cause. The commentary clarifies the terminology: 'essential cause' means no intermediate cause. It then explains the disagreement among physicians: some (like the Sheikh, possibly Avicenna) say both tafarruq al-ittisal and bad temperament are essential; Galen says only tafarruq al-ittisal is essential; al-Razi and later scholars say only bad temperament is essential, and tafarruq al-ittisal is a cause through that. The answer to al-Razi's objection is cut off, but it likely argues that the cut is so fast that the separation is not registered immediately.

السبب الذاتي
تفرق الاتصال
جالينوس
الإمام الرازي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All scholars agreed that tafarruq al-ittisal is the sole essential cause of pain.
Correction: The passage shows that Imam al-Razi denied this, implying disagreement. The commentary further explains three different positions among physicians.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do the philosophers claim as the essential cause of pain?
A) Heat and cold
B) Bad temperament
C) Tafarruq al-ittisal (discontinuity of continuity)
D) The striking of the eardrum

Key Takeaway
The philosophers and Galen argued that all pain ultimately results from discontinuity of continuity, but al-Razi's knife example raises a challenge that questions this sole causality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience pain, is your first thought to attribute it to a natural cause like a cut or illness, or does it drive you to seek Allah's wisdom and mercy in that trial? This lesson shows that even the best of philosophers disagreed about why pain occurs. How will you change your perspective to see pain as a direct communication from your Lord, not just a biological event?
?
Al-Razi's objection reveals that our knowledge of causes is limited and subject to doubt. Have you ever been so certain about your own understanding of a situation that you forgot that Allah alone knows the true reality? What does this teach you about intellectual humility before Allah's infinite knowledge?
?
The philosophers relied on experience (tajriba) to establish their claim. In your own life, have you placed too much trust in your experiences and observations, while neglecting the guidance of revelation? How can this lesson help you realign your priorities so that your faith informs your interpretation of every trial?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

First Proof: Temporal Delay Refutes Direct Causation
Presents the temporal delay proof and the non-existence argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the delay of pain after cutting disproves that discontinuity is a direct essential cause, and why non-existence cannot be the cause of an existent effect.

Core Idea
Delayed effect proves indirect causation.

In this passage, the author presents the first argument against the idea that discontinuity of connection (تفرق الاتصال) is a direct essential cause of pain. If cutting were a direct cause, pain would be immediate. But we observe a delay, so it must be an indirect cause. The Imam also argues that discontinuity is a non-existent thing (عدمي) and therefore cannot cause an existent thing like pain. This lesson explores these two key arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial argument
Read the first part of the passage that states if discontinuity were a direct cause, pain could not be delayed.
Notice the phrase 'لو كان ذلك سبباً ذاتياً قريباً لامتنع التخلف منه'.
2
Understand the temporal gap
The passage highlights that pain is absent after cutting, so discontinuity is not a direct cause but prepares the organ for a bad temperament.
The bad temperament is the actual cause of pain.
3
Examine the Imam's non-existence argument
Al-Imam argues that discontinuity is a non-existent thing (عدم الاتصال), and a non-existent cannot cause an existent effect.
This is a metaphysical principle.
4
Reflect on the significance
These arguments establish that the way we think about causation in material events must account for the nature of the cause.

النوع الخامس - المقصد الأول : اللذة والألم من بقية الكيفيات النفسانية
بعد زمان ولو كان ذلك سبباً ذاتياً قريباً لامتنع التخلف منه وحيث تخلف الألم عن القطع والتفريق ظهر أنه ليس سبباً كذلك بل تفرق الاتصال الحاصل بالقطع يعد العضو لسوء المزاج الذي هو الألم وحصوله يستدعي زماناً ما وإن كان قليلاً فربما يبتدئ العضو المقطوع بالاستحالة إلى مزاج سيء يحصل الألم الذي هو مسببه وربما احتج الإمام على ما أنكره من كون تفرق الاتصال سبباً ذاتياً للألم بأن التفرق عدم الاتصال عما شأنه أن يكون متصلاً وهو عدمي فلا يجوز أن يكون سبباً ذاتياً للألم الذي هو وجودي بالضرورة
من قوله : تفرق إما من داخل كخلط الحال أو محرق أو مرطب أو ميبس صارع أو متلازمي وخلطي وإما من خارج كجسم يمد وكالجبل أو يقطع كالسيف أو يحرق كالنار أو يرض كالحجر أو يثقب كالسهم أو بينهن كالكلب والأفعى والإنسان كذا في القانون قوله : لو كان ذلك سبباً ذاتياً لامتناع إلخ الملازمة ممنوعة لأن السبب الذاتي لا يلزم أن يكون علة موجبة حتى يمتنع التخلف عنه لجواز توقفه على شرط كيف والإمام يقول : إما سوء المزاج المخلف بسبب ذاتي للألم مع تخلفه عنه في حال عدم الشعور بالإغماء أو شرب دواء قوله : يعد العضو أي يهيأ لسوء المزاج وليس المراد به المغد الاصطلاحي لمجامعتهما قوله : بأن التفرق إلخ أجيب عنه بأن التفرق ليس عدم الاتصال بل حركة بعض الأجزاء عن بعض فلا يكون عدمياً والأولى أن يقال التفرق عبارة عن هيئة تحصل من بعد الأجزاء بعد الانفصال والحركة بدليل أن الألم باق بعد الحركة ولو كان التفرق حركة لزم أن يزول الألم بزوال الحركة
قوله: فلا يجوز أن يكون سبباً ذاتياً للألم الذي هو وجودي وقيل: العدمي يجوز أن يتصف به أمر في الخارج ويكون ذلك الأمر بسبب هذا الاتصاف موجباً للأمر الوجودي وفيه أنه خروج عن محل النزاع كما مر تقريره فيجب أن يألم المتغذي فيه أن التفرق الطبيعي غير مؤلم
التفات النفس والشعور فلا نُسَلّم تأخر الألم وإن كان بدونه فلا إشكال للاتفاق على أن الالتفات شرط ألا يرى أن من انصرف فكره إلى أمر أهم شريف كالتأمل في مسألة علمية أو خسيس كاللعب بالشطرنج وأمثالهما ربما لا يدرك ألم الجوع والعطش وأنت خبير بأن الفيصل في تأخر الألم التجربة
قوله : وهو عدمي فلا يجوز أن يكون إلخ أجيب بأن التفرق ليس عدم الاتصال بل حركة بعض الأجزاء عن البعض ولو سلم فالعدمي يجوز أن يتصف به أمر في الخارج ويكون ذلك الأمر هذا الاتصاف موجباً لأمر وجودي وبالجملة المراد بالسبب الذاتي هو الجزء الأخير من العلة التامة والأمر العدمي يجوز أن يكون جزءاً أخيراً مستلزماً للمعلول وإن لم يجز أن يكون بسبب موجوداً .
Fifth type - First intention: Pleasure and pain from the remaining psychic qualities. After a time, and if that (i.e., discontinuity of connection) were a proximate essential cause, then it would be impossible for it to be absent; but when pain is absent from cutting and separating, it appears that it is not a cause in that way. Rather, the discontinuity of connection resulting from cutting prepares the organ for the bad temperament which is the pain, and its occurrence requires some time, even if little. So perhaps the cut organ begins to transform into a bad temperament, and the pain which is its effect occurs. And perhaps the Imam argued, regarding what he denied that discontinuity of connection is an essential cause of pain, that discontinuity is the non-existence of connection from what should be connected, and it is non-existent, so it is not permissible for it to be an essential cause of pain, which is existent by necessity. From his saying: Discontinuity is either from inside, like the mixing of a humor or a burning, moistening, or drying agent, overwhelming or co-occurrent and humoral; or from outside, like a body that extends like a mountain, or cuts like a sword, or burns like fire, or crushes like a stone, or pierces like an arrow, or between them like a dog, a snake, and a human; thus in the Canon. His saying: If that were an essential cause, it would be impossible for it to be absent, etc. The implication is denied because the essential cause does not have to be an efficient cause such that it is impossible for it to be absent, due to the possibility of its depending on a condition. How? And the Imam says: Either the bad temperament arises from an essential cause of pain while pain is absent from it in the state of unconsciousness or drinking medicine. His saying: 'prepares the organ' means it makes it ready for the bad temperament, and the intended meaning is not the conventional 'stomach' because of their conjunction. His saying: 'that the discontinuity etc.' It is answered that discontinuity is not the non-existence of connection, but rather the movement of some parts away from others, so it is not non-existent; and it is better to say that discontinuity is a state that arises from the distance of parts after separation and movement, as evidenced by the fact that pain remains after movement; if discontinuity were movement, then pain would necessarily cease with the cessation of movement. His saying: 'So it is not permissible for it to be an essential cause of pain which is existent' and it was said: The non-existent thing may be characterized by something external, and that thing, due to this characterization, necessitates a existent thing; but it is said that this is a departure from the subject of dispute, as previously decided. So the nourished one must feel pain in it, because natural discontinuity is not painful. Attention of the soul and consciousness, so we do not concede the delay of pain; if it were without it, then there is no problem, by agreement that attention is a condition; do you not see that one whose thought is diverted to something more important, like reflection on a scientific problem, or base like playing chess and the like, perhaps does not perceive the pain of hunger and thirst; and you know well that the decisive factor in the delay of pain is experience. His saying: 'and it is non-existent so it is not permissible etc.' It is answered that discontinuity is not non-existence of connection, but rather movement of some parts from others; and even if it were granted, then the non-existent thing may be characterized by something external, and that thing, through this characterization, necessitates an existent thing. In summary, what is meant by essential cause is the final part of the complete cause, and the non-existent thing may be a final part entailing the effect, even if it is not permissible for it to be a cause as an existent thing.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a multi-layered proof and discussion concerning whether discontinuity of connection (تفرق الاتصال) is an essential cause (سبب ذاتي) of pain. The author begins by presenting the first proof: if discontinuity were a proximate essential cause, pain would necessarily follow immediately upon cutting. But pain is delayed, so discontinuity cannot be such a cause. Rather, it prepares the organ for a bad temperament, which is the actual cause of pain. The author also reports al-Imam's argument that discontinuity is a non-existent thing (عدم الاتصال), thus cannot cause an existent thing like pain. The sharh then clarifies types of discontinuity and addresses objections. An objection is raised that an essential cause may be conditional, so delay does not disprove it. The sharh responds by distinguishing that the Imam's argument is that the cause in question is either bad temperament (conditional) or discontinuity, and since pain is absent in some cases (unconsciousness), the condition is missing. Another objection claims discontinuity is not non-existence but movement, so it can cause existent pain; but the sharh counters that if movement were the cause, pain would cease when movement stops, yet pain remains. Finally, there is a discussion about the role of attention and consciousness as a condition for perceiving pain, and the final response reiterates that the essential cause is the final part of the complete cause, which can include non-existent things as parts.

Key Terms
تفرق الاتصال
سبب ذاتي
عدم الاتصال
عدمي
وجودي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the delay of pain proves discontinuity has no causal role whatsoever.
Correction: The passage only disproves direct causation; discontinuity still acts as a preparatory condition for the bad temperament that causes pain.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason given in the passage that discontinuity cannot be a direct essential cause of pain?
A) Because pain occurs instantly upon cutting.
B) Because pain is delayed after cutting.
C) Because discontinuity is a non-existent thing.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
The delay of pain and the non-existence of discontinuity together show that it is not a direct cause, but rather a preparatory condition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience pain or hardship, do you immediately assume a direct cause you can point to? This lesson teaches that even physical pain is not directly caused by the obvious trigger but by a hidden process (bad temperament). How often do you seek the deeper, hidden causes of your struggles rather than blaming the surface event? Will you commit, starting today, to pause and reflect before attributing causality?
?
The author argues that non-existence (like a severed connection) cannot bring existence (pain) into being. In your spiritual life, have you ever assumed that an absence—like lack of time, lack of resources, or lack of opportunity—is the direct reason for your failure to act righteously? How does this lesson challenge you to reconsider those excuses? What will you change?
?
The delay of pain after cutting reveals that the cause is not the cut itself but a preparation of the organ. Think of a recent trial you faced: did you immediately judge its cause as the apparent event? Could Allah be preparing you through that event for something deeper? How will you train yourself to wait for the spiritual 'bad temperament' to manifest before concluding the cause?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
Objections to the Non-Existence Argument and Final Clarification
Addresses objections and provides final clarifications on the nature of essential cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various objections raised against the non-existence argument, including the role of attention and the definition of essential cause as the final part of the complete cause.

Core Idea
Essential cause can include non-existence as a part.

After the initial proof, the text addresses objections. Some argue that an essential cause does not have to be an efficient cause; it may depend on conditions. Others claim discontinuity is not non-existence but movement, so it can cause pain. There is also a discussion about the role of attention and consciousness in perceiving pain. Finally, the essential cause is redefined as the final part of the complete cause, which can include non-existent elements. This lesson unpacks these nuanced responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection about essential cause
The passage presents an objection that an essential cause may not be an efficient cause; it may require a condition, so delay does not disprove it.
The response distinguishes between direct cause and conditional cause.
2
Examine the movement argument
Another objection claims discontinuity is movement (a existent thing), not non-existence. The response counters that pain persists after movement stops, so movement cannot be the cause.
This shows the cause is not movement but something else.
3
Understand the role of attention
The passage notes that pain perception requires attention; if attention is diverted, pain is not felt, questioning the delay.
This suggests that delay may be due to lack of attention, not absence of cause.
4
Final definition of essential cause
The essential cause is defined as the final part of the complete cause, which can include non-existent things as conditions.
This reframes the debate and allows non-existent parts.

النوع الخامس - المقصد الأول : اللذة والألم من بقية الكيفيات النفسانية
بعد زمان ولو كان ذلك سبباً ذاتياً قريباً لامتنع التخلف منه وحيث تخلف الألم عن القطع والتفريق ظهر أنه ليس سبباً كذلك بل تفرق الاتصال الحاصل بالقطع يعد العضو لسوء المزاج الذي هو الألم وحصوله يستدعي زماناً ما وإن كان قليلاً فربما يبتدئ العضو المقطوع بالاستحالة إلى مزاج سيء يحصل الألم الذي هو مسببه وربما احتج الإمام على ما أنكره من كون تفرق الاتصال سبباً ذاتياً للألم بأن التفرق عدم الاتصال عما شأنه أن يكون متصلاً وهو عدمي فلا يجوز أن يكون سبباً ذاتياً للألم الذي هو وجودي بالضرورة
من قوله : تفرق إما من داخل كخلط الحال أو محرق أو مرطب أو ميبس صارع أو متلازمي وخلطي وإما من خارج كجسم يمد وكالجبل أو يقطع كالسيف أو يحرق كالنار أو يرض كالحجر أو يثقب كالسهم أو بينهن كالكلب والأفعى والإنسان كذا في القانون قوله : لو كان ذلك سبباً ذاتياً لامتناع إلخ الملازمة ممنوعة لأن السبب الذاتي لا يلزم أن يكون علة موجبة حتى يمتنع التخلف عنه لجواز توقفه على شرط كيف والإمام يقول : إما سوء المزاج المخلف بسبب ذاتي للألم مع تخلفه عنه في حال عدم الشعور بالإغماء أو شرب دواء قوله : يعد العضو أي يهيأ لسوء المزاج وليس المراد به المغد الاصطلاحي لمجامعتهما قوله : بأن التفرق إلخ أجيب عنه بأن التفرق ليس عدم الاتصال بل حركة بعض الأجزاء عن بعض فلا يكون عدمياً والأولى أن يقال التفرق عبارة عن هيئة تحصل من بعد الأجزاء بعد الانفصال والحركة بدليل أن الألم باق بعد الحركة ولو كان التفرق حركة لزم أن يزول الألم بزوال الحركة
قوله: فلا يجوز أن يكون سبباً ذاتياً للألم الذي هو وجودي وقيل: العدمي يجوز أن يتصف به أمر في الخارج ويكون ذلك الأمر بسبب هذا الاتصاف موجباً للأمر الوجودي وفيه أنه خروج عن محل النزاع كما مر تقريره فيجب أن يألم المتغذي فيه أن التفرق الطبيعي غير مؤلم

According to the passage, what is the final definition of an essential cause?
A) An efficient cause that necessitates its effect immediately.
B) The final part of the complete cause, which can include non-existent things.
C) The movement of parts that produces pain.
D) A cause that requires attention to be effective.

?

?

Causality and Created Things
